# Building Great Forms with HTML and CSS

> While creating forms with HTML and CSS is a core web development skill, making great forms requires a lot more than the basics. Emily Kay brings her years of experience working with customers and developers to explain how to create forms people will find useful and even enjoyable. Learn how to set up a basic form and add buttons, toggle switches, date pickers, and more. Become well-versed in form-

> [LinkedIn Learning](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/building-great-forms-with-html-and-css) | 6h 59m | Intermediate | 11K learners

> **[0:57](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/building-great-forms-with-html-and-css/making-your-forms-great?u=76281980&t=57)** We can improve usability for everyone by harnessing the power of our computer and some code to make form filling easier.

>
> **[1:16](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/building-great-forms-with-html-and-css/making-your-forms-great?u=76281980&t=76)** All of these are operations that you can have your computer take care of simply by using the right code.
>
> **[1:22](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/building-great-forms-with-html-and-css/making-your-forms-great?u=76281980&t=82)** This elevates the responsibility from the end user.

### 1. General Form Setup

#### How HTML forms work
> [LinkedIn Learning](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/building-great-forms-with-html-and-css/how-html-forms-work?u=76281980)

> [!transcript]- Transcript
>
> **[0:00](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/building-great-forms-with-html-and-css/how-html-forms-work?u=76281980&t=0)** - [Instructor] While other elements of HTML gives style and meaning to your website, an HTML form adds interactivity.
>
> **[0:07](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/building-great-forms-with-html-and-css/how-html-forms-work?u=76281980&t=7)** It allows you to communicate with your end users and provides a method to make the web dynamic.
>
> **[0:13](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/building-great-forms-with-html-and-css/how-html-forms-work?u=76281980&t=13)** HTML forms handle important functions like taking orders, surveys, user registration and more.
>
> **[0:20](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/building-great-forms-with-html-and-css/how-html-forms-work?u=76281980&t=20)** You will hardly find a single website without some sort of form of it.
>
> **[0:24](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/building-great-forms-with-html-and-css/how-html-forms-work?u=76281980&t=24)** HTML forms are one of the main points of interaction between a user and a website or application.
>
> **[0:30](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/building-great-forms-with-html-and-css/how-html-forms-work?u=76281980&t=30)** They allow us to connect with our users.
>
> **[0:33](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/building-great-forms-with-html-and-css/how-html-forms-work?u=76281980&t=33)** An HTML form is made up of one or more widgets.
>
> **[0:36](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/building-great-forms-with-html-and-css/how-html-forms-work?u=76281980&t=36)** Those widgets can be single or multi-line text fields, select boxes, checkboxes, radio buttons, or buttons.
>
> **[0:44](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/building-great-forms-with-html-and-css/how-html-forms-work?u=76281980&t=44)** The single line text field widget can even require data entered to be of a specific format or value.
>
> **[0:50](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/building-great-forms-with-html-and-css/how-html-forms-work?u=76281980&t=50)** Form widgets should be paired with a properly implemented label that describes their purpose.
>
> **[0:56](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/building-great-forms-with-html-and-css/how-html-forms-work?u=76281980&t=56)** Labels help instruct both sighted and blind users on what to enter into a form input.
>
> **[1:01](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/building-great-forms-with-html-and-css/how-html-forms-work?u=76281980&t=61)** The main difference between an HTML form and a regular HTML document is that most of the time, the data collected by the form is sent to a web server.
>
> **[1:10](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/building-great-forms-with-html-and-css/how-html-forms-work?u=76281980&t=70)** In that case, you need to setup a web server to receive and process the data.
>
> **[1:15](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/building-great-forms-with-html-and-css/how-html-forms-work?u=76281980&t=75)** In this course, we will be focusing on the front end build out of web forms, but I think it is important to outline the steps of how a form functions with the backend.
>
> **[1:25](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/building-great-forms-with-html-and-css/how-html-forms-work?u=76281980&t=85)** Let's take a look.
>
> **[1:26](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/building-great-forms-with-html-and-css/how-html-forms-work?u=76281980&t=86)** A web form has two parts, the HTML front end, which is the client-side HTML, and a backend form processor, which is the server-side script.
>
> **[1:37](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/building-great-forms-with-html-and-css/how-html-forms-work?u=76281980&t=97)** The HTML front end part handles the presentation, that's what you see in your browser, while the backend handles the form submission, like saving the form submission or sending emails.
>
> **[1:48](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/building-great-forms-with-html-and-css/how-html-forms-work?u=76281980&t=108)** A visitor visits a webpage that contains a form.
>
> **[1:52](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/building-great-forms-with-html-and-css/how-html-forms-work?u=76281980&t=112)** The web browser displays the HTML form.
>
> **[1:55](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/building-great-forms-with-html-and-css/how-html-forms-work?u=76281980&t=115)** The visitor fills in the form and submits.
>
> **[1:58](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/building-great-forms-with-html-and-css/how-html-forms-work?u=76281980&t=118)** The browser sends the submitted data to the web server.
>
> **[2:02](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/building-great-forms-with-html-and-css/how-html-forms-work?u=76281980&t=122)** A form processor script running on the web server processes that form data.
>
> **[2:06](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/building-great-forms-with-html-and-css/how-html-forms-work?u=76281980&t=126)** The processing steps can include sending the form submission by email or saving the submission to a database table or a file.
>
> **[2:14](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/building-great-forms-with-html-and-css/how-html-forms-work?u=76281980&t=134)** Then a response page is sent back to the browser.
>
> **[2:17](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/building-great-forms-with-html-and-css/how-html-forms-work?u=76281980&t=137)** Client-side code cannot do processing like sending email or saving to a database, so in order for a form to work, both of these parts need to be present in order for it to function properly.
>
> **[2:29](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/building-great-forms-with-html-and-css/how-html-forms-work?u=76281980&t=149)** Again, in this course, we will focus on the front end scripting, HTML, CSS, and some JavaScript.
>
> **[2:36](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/building-great-forms-with-html-and-css/how-html-forms-work?u=76281980&t=156)** I will show you how to layout and design usable and great looking forms.

> [!info]- Semantic Content
>
> **Env Vars:** html (12), css (1)
> **Code Keywords:** require (1), case, (1), let (1), function (1)
> **CLI Commands:** make (1), find (1)
> **Prerequisites:** setup (1)
> **Speakers:** - [instructor] (1)

#### Basic setup for a form
> [LinkedIn Learning](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/building-great-forms-with-html-and-css/basic-setup-for-a-form?u=76281980)

> [!transcript]- Transcript
>
> **[0:00](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/building-great-forms-with-html-and-css/basic-setup-for-a-form?u=76281980&t=0)** - [Instructor] In order to build out a form, we start by using good old semantic HTML.
>
> **[0:05](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/building-great-forms-with-html-and-css/basic-setup-for-a-form?u=76281980&t=5)** The form itself resides inside of a form tag.
>
> **[0:09](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/building-great-forms-with-html-and-css/basic-setup-for-a-form?u=76281980&t=9)** Typically, the form tag contains a number of form-associated elements that are nested between the start and the end of the form tags.
>
> **[0:18](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/building-great-forms-with-html-and-css/basic-setup-for-a-form?u=76281980&t=18)** These are things like labels, input tags, check boxes, select lists, et cetera.
>
> **[0:26](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/building-great-forms-with-html-and-css/basic-setup-for-a-form?u=76281980&t=26)** The form tag usually has an action attribute specified, which is going to identify the page that will process the form.
>
> **[0:33](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/building-great-forms-with-html-and-css/basic-setup-for-a-form?u=76281980&t=33)** This also designates where to send the form data.
>
> **[0:37](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/building-great-forms-with-html-and-css/basic-setup-for-a-form?u=76281980&t=37)** Its value needs to be a valid relative or absolute URL.
>
> **[0:42](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/building-great-forms-with-html-and-css/basic-setup-for-a-form?u=76281980&t=42)** If this attribute isn't provided, the data will be sent to the URL of the page containing the form, which is the current page that you're on.
>
> **[0:50](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/building-great-forms-with-html-and-css/basic-setup-for-a-form?u=76281980&t=50)** We also need the method attribute.
>
> **[0:53](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/building-great-forms-with-html-and-css/basic-setup-for-a-form?u=76281980&t=53)** This is going to specify how to send the form data to the web server.
>
> **[0:57](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/building-great-forms-with-html-and-css/basic-setup-for-a-form?u=76281980&t=57)** The HTTP protocol provides several ways to perform a request, the most common being the GET method and the POST method.
>
> **[1:06](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/building-great-forms-with-html-and-css/basic-setup-for-a-form?u=76281980&t=66)** We'll be looking at those next.

> [!info]- Semantic Content
>
> **Env Vars:** url (2), html (1), http (1), post (1)
> **API Endpoints:** get  (1), post  (1)
> **Speakers:** - [instructor] (1)

#### GET and POST methods
> [LinkedIn Learning](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/building-great-forms-with-html-and-css/get-and-post-methods?u=76281980)

> [!transcript]- Transcript
>
> **[0:00](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/building-great-forms-with-html-and-css/get-and-post-methods?u=76281980&t=0)** - [Instructor] To understand the difference between GET and POST, let's step back and examine how HTTP works.
>
> **[0:07](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/building-great-forms-with-html-and-css/get-and-post-methods?u=76281980&t=7)** Each time you want to reach a resource on the web, the browser sends a request to a URL.
>
> **[0:12](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/building-great-forms-with-html-and-css/get-and-post-methods?u=76281980&t=12)** An HTTP request consists of two parts, a header, that contains a set of global metadata about the browser's capabilities, and a body that can contain information necessary for the server to process this specific request.
>
> **[0:27](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/building-great-forms-with-html-and-css/get-and-post-methods?u=76281980&t=27)** The GET method is the method used by the browser to ask the server to send back a given resource.
>
> **[0:33](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/building-great-forms-with-html-and-css/get-and-post-methods?u=76281980&t=33)** It says, "Hey server, I want to get this resource".
>
> **[0:37](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/building-great-forms-with-html-and-css/get-and-post-methods?u=76281980&t=37)** In this case, the browser sends an empty body.
>
> **[0:40](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/building-great-forms-with-html-and-css/get-and-post-methods?u=76281980&t=40)** Because the body is empty, If a form is sent using this method, the data sent to the server is appended to the URL.
>
> **[0:48](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/building-great-forms-with-html-and-css/get-and-post-methods?u=76281980&t=48)** GET allows the browser to cache the results of the form submission, and it also allows the user to bookmark the page once the form has been submitted.
>
> **[0:57](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/building-great-forms-with-html-and-css/get-and-post-methods?u=76281980&t=57)** However, GET is limited in the amount of content in the submission, and it is not secure since the info is simply appended to the browser's URL.
>
> **[1:06](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/building-great-forms-with-html-and-css/get-and-post-methods?u=76281980&t=66)** As such, GET is generally used for simple web forms where security is not a concern.
>
> **[1:13](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/building-great-forms-with-html-and-css/get-and-post-methods?u=76281980&t=73)** Let's take a look at how GET works.
>
> **[1:15](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/building-great-forms-with-html-and-css/get-and-post-methods?u=76281980&t=75)** Here's our simple contact form.
>
> **[1:18](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/building-great-forms-with-html-and-css/get-and-post-methods?u=76281980&t=78)** We're going to go into the form element, and we're going to change the method attribute to have a value of GET.
>
> **[1:25](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/building-great-forms-with-html-and-css/get-and-post-methods?u=76281980&t=85)** I'll save the page and switch out to the browser.
>
> **[1:28](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/building-great-forms-with-html-and-css/get-and-post-methods?u=76281980&t=88)** And before I go ahead and fill in the form, I want to bring your attention to the address bar in the URL.
>
> **[1:34](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/building-great-forms-with-html-and-css/get-and-post-methods?u=76281980&t=94)** So take note of what the address bar currently looks like.
>
> **[1:38](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/building-great-forms-with-html-and-css/get-and-post-methods?u=76281980&t=98)** We'll go ahead and we'll fill out the form.
>
> **[1:41](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/building-great-forms-with-html-and-css/get-and-post-methods?u=76281980&t=101)** And when I click contact us, keep your eye on the address bar.
>
> **[1:45](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/building-great-forms-with-html-and-css/get-and-post-methods?u=76281980&t=105)** You're going to see that the address bar is going to be appended with the results of the form.
>
> **[1:52](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/building-great-forms-with-html-and-css/get-and-post-methods?u=76281980&t=112)** The data is appended to the URL as a series of name-value pairs.
>
> **[1:58](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/building-great-forms-with-html-and-css/get-and-post-methods?u=76281980&t=118)** After the URL web address has ended, we include a question mark, followed by the name-value pairs.
>
> **[2:05](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/building-great-forms-with-html-and-css/get-and-post-methods?u=76281980&t=125)** Each one is separated by an ampersand.
>
> **[2:08](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/building-great-forms-with-html-and-css/get-and-post-methods?u=76281980&t=128)** In this case, we're passing two pieces of data to the server, of name and the email address.
>
> **[2:15](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/building-great-forms-with-html-and-css/get-and-post-methods?u=76281980&t=135)** You'll see that on the web browser, it says form is not submitted.
>
> **[2:19](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/building-great-forms-with-html-and-css/get-and-post-methods?u=76281980&t=139)** And that's because we're still pointing to the process.php page, which is actually counting on the fact that we would be using the POST method.
>
> **[2:28](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/building-great-forms-with-html-and-css/get-and-post-methods?u=76281980&t=148)** Let's go ahead and talk about POST next.
>
> **[2:31](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/building-great-forms-with-html-and-css/get-and-post-methods?u=76281980&t=151)** The POST method is a little bit different.
>
> **[2:33](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/building-great-forms-with-html-and-css/get-and-post-methods?u=76281980&t=153)** It's the method the browser uses to talk to the server when asking for a response, and takes into account the data provided in the body of the HTTP request.
>
> **[2:43](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/building-great-forms-with-html-and-css/get-and-post-methods?u=76281980&t=163)** It says, "Hey server, take a look at this data "and send me back an appropriate result."
>
> **[2:49](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/building-great-forms-with-html-and-css/get-and-post-methods?u=76281980&t=169)** Specifying a value of POST means the browser will send the data to the web server to be processed.
>
> **[2:55](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/building-great-forms-with-html-and-css/get-and-post-methods?u=76281980&t=175)** This is necessary when adding data to a database or when submitting sensitive information such as passwords.
>
> **[3:02](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/building-great-forms-with-html-and-css/get-and-post-methods?u=76281980&t=182)** POST appends form data inside the body of the HTTP request.
>
> **[3:07](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/building-great-forms-with-html-and-css/get-and-post-methods?u=76281980&t=187)** The data will not be shown in the URL.
>
> **[3:09](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/building-great-forms-with-html-and-css/get-and-post-methods?u=76281980&t=189)** POST has no size limits and is much more secure.
>
> **[3:13](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/building-great-forms-with-html-and-css/get-and-post-methods?u=76281980&t=193)** Typically, you will use this more often when building out web forms.
>
> **[3:17](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/building-great-forms-with-html-and-css/get-and-post-methods?u=76281980&t=197)** Let's take a look at how POST works.
>
> **[3:20](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/building-great-forms-with-html-and-css/get-and-post-methods?u=76281980&t=200)** I'm going to go ahead and I'm going to change the method to POST.
>
> **[3:24](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/building-great-forms-with-html-and-css/get-and-post-methods?u=76281980&t=204)** We'll save our page and go look at it in the browser.
>
> **[3:28](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/building-great-forms-with-html-and-css/get-and-post-methods?u=76281980&t=208)** I'm going to fill out the fields.

>
> **[4:00](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/building-great-forms-with-html-and-css/get-and-post-methods?u=76281980&t=240)** And if POST has been submitted, and the information was not filled out, we get that form is not submitted.
>
> **[4:07](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/building-great-forms-with-html-and-css/get-and-post-methods?u=76281980&t=247)** So that's what you saw when we use the GET method.
>
> **[4:09](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/building-great-forms-with-html-and-css/get-and-post-methods?u=76281980&t=249)** Since this statement was not true, it went ahead and echoed out form is not submitted.
>
> **[4:16](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/building-great-forms-with-html-and-css/get-and-post-methods?u=76281980&t=256)** But, when we use POST, it goes ahead and it checks the name and the email fields.
>
> **[4:22](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/building-great-forms-with-html-and-css/get-and-post-methods?u=76281980&t=262)** And it finds out if those have been filled out.
>
> **[4:24](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/building-great-forms-with-html-and-css/get-and-post-methods?u=76281980&t=264)** If they have, it's going to go ahead and mail the results to the specified email, as well as redirect the user to an additional page.
>
> **[4:34](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/building-great-forms-with-html-and-css/get-and-post-methods?u=76281980&t=274)** In this case, it's our thank you page.
>
> **[4:37](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/building-great-forms-with-html-and-css/get-and-post-methods?u=76281980&t=277)** PHP offers some global objects which allow us to access the data.
>
> **[4:42](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/building-great-forms-with-html-and-css/get-and-post-methods?u=76281980&t=282)** Because we're using the POST method in this example, it's taking the data and it's emailing it to me, and then redirecting.
>
> **[4:49](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/building-great-forms-with-html-and-css/get-and-post-methods?u=76281980&t=289)** Of course, what you do with the data is up to you.

>
> **[4:59](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/building-great-forms-with-html-and-css/get-and-post-methods?u=76281980&t=299)** Now that you have idea of the overall picture of how form data is processed, we can begin to build out some of our forms using our front end languages like HTML, CSS, and JavaScript.

### 2. Form Elements

#### Fieldsets and legends
> [LinkedIn Learning](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/building-great-forms-with-html-and-css/fieldsets-and-legends?u=76281980)

> [!transcript]- Transcript
>
> **[0:00](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/building-great-forms-with-html-and-css/fieldsets-and-legends?u=76281980&t=0)** - [Instructor] While not necessary in every form, fieldsets and legends are useful in larger forms and can help to organize the fields and guide users and aid in accessibility.
>
> **[0:11](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/building-great-forms-with-html-and-css/fieldsets-and-legends?u=76281980&t=11)** The fieldset and the legend elements work together to tell screen readers that a group of form fields relate to each other and to provide a label for that particular group.
>
> **[0:20](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/building-great-forms-with-html-and-css/fieldsets-and-legends?u=76281980&t=20)** They can also visually help to separate and organize content within a page and help to ensure that your page is more semantic.
>
> **[0:28](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/building-great-forms-with-html-and-css/fieldsets-and-legends?u=76281980&t=28)** The fieldset element provides a container for related form controls and the legend element acts as a heading to identify the group.
>
> **[0:36](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/building-great-forms-with-html-and-css/fieldsets-and-legends?u=76281980&t=36)** All of the related fields go inside the fieldset element and the legend element is used to represent the overarching label for that particular group.
>
> **[0:45](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/building-great-forms-with-html-and-css/fieldsets-and-legends?u=76281980&t=45)** These are especially useful when you're working with radio buttons and checkboxes as they provide a method for grouping these elements together.
>
> **[0:53](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/building-great-forms-with-html-and-css/fieldsets-and-legends?u=76281980&t=53)** You should use the fieldset and legend elements when you have a single multiple choice question as in you're using radio buttons or checkboxes or you have several questions relating to the same topic, like text boxes or any other type of field.
>
> **[1:08](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/building-great-forms-with-html-and-css/fieldsets-and-legends?u=76281980&t=68)** When you wish to organize your form into related groups of content then you'll definitely want to use fieldset and legend.
>

#### Working with labels
> [LinkedIn Learning](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/building-great-forms-with-html-and-css/working-with-labels?u=76281980)

> [!transcript]- Transcript
>
> **[0:00](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/building-great-forms-with-html-and-css/working-with-labels?u=76281980&t=0)** - [Instructor] A label is used to create a caption for a form control.
>
> **[0:04](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/building-great-forms-with-html-and-css/working-with-labels?u=76281980&t=4)** And they describe the purpose and function of form elements.
>
> **[0:08](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/building-great-forms-with-html-and-css/working-with-labels?u=76281980&t=8)** Labels are critical because they tell the user what information to provide in the form element.
>
> **[0:13](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/building-great-forms-with-html-and-css/working-with-labels?u=76281980&t=13)** Without them we really would be lost.
>
> **[0:16](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/building-great-forms-with-html-and-css/working-with-labels?u=76281980&t=16)** Clearly written labels are one of the primary ways to make a UI more accessible.
>
> **[0:21](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/building-great-forms-with-html-and-css/working-with-labels?u=76281980&t=21)** A good label tells the user the purpose of the field, maintains its usefulness when focus is on the field itself and remains visible even after the field has been filled in.
>
> **[0:32](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/building-great-forms-with-html-and-css/working-with-labels?u=76281980&t=32)** The label text is not only visually associated with its corresponding text input, it is also programmatically associated with it.
>
> **[0:40](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/building-great-forms-with-html-and-css/working-with-labels?u=76281980&t=40)** This means that for example, a screen reader will read out the label when the user is focused on the form input, making it easier for an assistive technology user to understand what data should be entered.
>
> **[0:53](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/building-great-forms-with-html-and-css/working-with-labels?u=76281980&t=53)** You can click the associated label to focus the active input as well as clicking the input itself.
>
> **[1:00](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/building-great-forms-with-html-and-css/working-with-labels?u=76281980&t=60)** The increased hit area provides an advantage to anyone trying to activate the input, including those with touch-based devices.
>
> **[1:08](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/building-great-forms-with-html-and-css/working-with-labels?u=76281980&t=68)** In this example, the label is linked to the input field.
>
> **[1:11](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/building-great-forms-with-html-and-css/working-with-labels?u=76281980&t=71)** Here I have text that is not linked to the label.
>
> **[1:15](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/building-great-forms-with-html-and-css/working-with-labels?u=76281980&t=75)** And if I click on this text, nothing happens.
>
> **[1:18](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/building-great-forms-with-html-and-css/working-with-labels?u=76281980&t=78)** I would only be able to click within the input field.
>
> **[1:21](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/building-great-forms-with-html-and-css/working-with-labels?u=76281980&t=81)** So in this example, even though they look very similar, the label is not programmatically associated with the field itself.
>
> **[1:30](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/building-great-forms-with-html-and-css/working-with-labels?u=76281980&t=90)** There are four broad parameters that improve the user experience for everyone.
>
> **[1:35](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/building-great-forms-with-html-and-css/working-with-labels?u=76281980&t=95)** Visual, make it easy to see.
>
> **[1:38](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/building-great-forms-with-html-and-css/working-with-labels?u=76281980&t=98)** Auditory, make it easy to hear.
>
> **[1:41](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/building-great-forms-with-html-and-css/working-with-labels?u=76281980&t=101)** Motor, make it easy to interact with.
>
> **[1:44](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/building-great-forms-with-html-and-css/working-with-labels?u=76281980&t=104)** And cognitive, make it easy to understand.
>
> **[1:47](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/building-great-forms-with-html-and-css/working-with-labels?u=76281980&t=107)** By looking at labels from each of these standpoints.
>
> **[1:50](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/building-great-forms-with-html-and-css/working-with-labels?u=76281980&t=110)** We can see just how important labels are.
>
> **[1:52](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/building-great-forms-with-html-and-css/working-with-labels?u=76281980&t=112)** Sighted users can read them, visually impaired users can hear them by using a screen reader and motor impaired users can more easily set focus to the field thanks to the larger hit area.
>
> **[2:04](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/building-great-forms-with-html-and-css/working-with-labels?u=76281980&t=124)** Having labels makes our form easy for everyone to understand and utilize the form.
>
> **[2:09](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/building-great-forms-with-html-and-css/working-with-labels?u=76281980&t=129)** There are two ways to incorporate labels into your forms.
>
> **[2:12](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/building-great-forms-with-html-and-css/working-with-labels?u=76281980&t=132)** Explicitly and implicitly.
>
> **[2:15](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/building-great-forms-with-html-and-css/working-with-labels?u=76281980&t=135)** Let's take a look at what both of those are.
>
> **[2:18](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/building-great-forms-with-html-and-css/working-with-labels?u=76281980&t=138)** In the example here, I have a fieldset with a legend that says explicit labels.
>
> **[2:23](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/building-great-forms-with-html-and-css/working-with-labels?u=76281980&t=143)** These labels are all explicitly associated with the input element.
>
> **[2:28](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/building-great-forms-with-html-and-css/working-with-labels?u=76281980&t=148)** We create inexplicit association by giving the input an ID attribute.
>
> **[2:35](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/building-great-forms-with-html-and-css/working-with-labels?u=76281980&t=155)** The label then needs a for attribute whose value is the same as the inputs ID.
>
> **[2:42](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/building-great-forms-with-html-and-css/working-with-labels?u=76281980&t=162)** In the HTML, the label is linked to the input field using the ID attribute of the input field and the for attribute of the label field.
>
> **[2:51](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/building-great-forms-with-html-and-css/working-with-labels?u=76281980&t=171)** If we take a peek in the browser to see what this looks like, here are my explicitly associated labels.
>
> **[2:58](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/building-great-forms-with-html-and-css/working-with-labels?u=76281980&t=178)** Now I've provided some CSS formatting to ensure that the labels are on top of the input field.
>
> **[3:04](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/building-great-forms-with-html-and-css/working-with-labels?u=76281980&t=184)** By default, they would not appear that way, as labels themselves are inline elements.
>
> **[3:10](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/building-great-forms-with-html-and-css/working-with-labels?u=76281980&t=190)** Alternatively, you can nest inputs directly inside the label.
>
> **[3:14](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/building-great-forms-with-html-and-css/working-with-labels?u=76281980&t=194)** In which case the for and the ID attributes are not needed because the association is implicit.
>
> **[3:21](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/building-great-forms-with-html-and-css/working-with-labels?u=76281980&t=201)** However, there are some older technologies that may not associate the label with the form control.
>
> **[3:27](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/building-great-forms-with-html-and-css/working-with-labels?u=76281980&t=207)** For this reason, the for and the ID attributes still should be used, so that there's an explicit relationship.
>
> **[3:34](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/building-great-forms-with-html-and-css/working-with-labels?u=76281980&t=214)** As you can see here, the label tag is wrapping around the input tag.
>
> **[3:39](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/building-great-forms-with-html-and-css/working-with-labels?u=76281980&t=219)** This is what creates the implicit relationship.
>
> **[3:42](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/building-great-forms-with-html-and-css/working-with-labels?u=76281980&t=222)** I like to use the implicit labels on radio buttons and checkboxes because it ensures the greatest breath of support when it comes to styling inputs.
>
> **[3:52](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/building-great-forms-with-html-and-css/working-with-labels?u=76281980&t=232)** In regards to which option you should use, it's really dependent on your layout and the needs of your design.
>
> **[3:58](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/building-great-forms-with-html-and-css/working-with-labels?u=76281980&t=238)** Let's save our page and let's look at this in the browser.
>
> **[4:01](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/building-great-forms-with-html-and-css/working-with-labels?u=76281980&t=241)** As you can see, my implicit labels are going to appear next to the input fields.
>
> **[4:07](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/building-great-forms-with-html-and-css/working-with-labels?u=76281980&t=247)** As opposed to having the label on top.
>
> **[4:10](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/building-great-forms-with-html-and-css/working-with-labels?u=76281980&t=250)** Now you can easily style this using CSS, except if the label is implicit it's going to be a little tougher to get the label to be on top of the input field as is shown with my explicit labels.
>
> **[4:23](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/building-great-forms-with-html-and-css/working-with-labels?u=76281980&t=263)** In order to improve usability, we generally want the label to be on top of the input field in most situations.
>
> **[4:31](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/building-great-forms-with-html-and-css/working-with-labels?u=76281980&t=271)** This is true for all elements, except for radio buttons and checkboxes.
>
> **[4:37](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/building-great-forms-with-html-and-css/working-with-labels?u=76281980&t=277)** With these form controls, the label content comes after the form control.
>
> **[4:42](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/building-great-forms-with-html-and-css/working-with-labels?u=76281980&t=282)** So the implicit label makes more sense.
>
> **[4:45](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/building-great-forms-with-html-and-css/working-with-labels?u=76281980&t=285)** If we check back in our code, you can see I've created the label tag, I've nested the input tag inside of the opening and closing label tag and the text comes after the input tag.
>
> **[4:57](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/building-great-forms-with-html-and-css/working-with-labels?u=76281980&t=297)** If you compare this to what I have above, in which case I'm using implicit labels.
>
> **[5:02](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/building-great-forms-with-html-and-css/working-with-labels?u=76281980&t=302)** I have the label, the text and then the input tag.
>
> **[5:05](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/building-great-forms-with-html-and-css/working-with-labels?u=76281980&t=305)** So think about what you're trying to accomplish and what makes the most sense to your end users.
>
> **[5:11](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/building-great-forms-with-html-and-css/working-with-labels?u=76281980&t=311)** It's worth noting that the input type of submit, reset or buttons aren't going to need a label.
>
> **[5:18](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/building-great-forms-with-html-and-css/working-with-labels?u=76281980&t=318)** Labels are really one of the most important elements if you want to build accessible forms.
>
> **[5:23](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/building-great-forms-with-html-and-css/working-with-labels?u=76281980&t=323)** When implemented properly, screen readers will speak the forms element label along with any related instructions.
>
> **[5:30](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/building-great-forms-with-html-and-css/working-with-labels?u=76281980&t=330)** They also provide necessary feedback to visual end-users as well.

#### New HTML5 form tags

> **[0:00](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/building-great-forms-with-html-and-css/new-html5-form-tags?u=76281980&t=0)** - [Instructor] As of HTML5, we really only gained two new form elements, but we did gain a slew of attributes and new types of input elements.
>
> **[0:09](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/building-great-forms-with-html-and-css/new-html5-form-tags?u=76281980&t=9)** More on this soon.

> **[0:16](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/building-great-forms-with-html-and-css/new-html5-form-tags?u=76281980&t=16)** The Datalist element not only allows the user to view a suggested list of values that appear in an associated input field, but the user can also type in their own values.

>
> **[1:48](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/building-great-forms-with-html-and-css/new-html5-form-tags?u=76281980&t=108)** Once the input field is done, I'll go ahead and create the datalist, and the datalist element is going to need to have an ID attribute that matches the list attribute that we created in the input field, so the list attribute value and the ID value are going to need to match.
>
> **[2:10](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/building-great-forms-with-html-and-css/new-html5-form-tags?u=76281980&t=130)** This is how we link these items together.

> **[3:03](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/building-great-forms-with-html-and-css/new-html5-form-tags?u=76281980&t=183)** So this gives the user a little bit more flexibilty, they can choose from a list of suggestions, or they can go ahead and put their own value in there.
>
> **[3:16](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/building-great-forms-with-html-and-css/new-html5-form-tags?u=76281980&t=196)** Datalists provide a quick native means of displaying input suggestions to our user, and since these options are merely suggestions, for many situations, it's not even necessary to provide any sort fallback for unsupporting browsers since these browsers will just not see the suggestions, and the field will just appear as an input field.
>
> **[3:37](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/building-great-forms-with-html-and-css/new-html5-form-tags?u=76281980&t=217)** The other new element that we have is the output element.
>
> **[3:41](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/building-great-forms-with-html-and-css/new-html5-form-tags?u=76281980&t=221)** The output tag is used for representing the result of a calculation from a user action.
>
> **[3:47](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/building-great-forms-with-html-and-css/new-html5-form-tags?u=76281980&t=227)** This could include displaying the result of a calculation performed by a script, or it could display the output of a user's interaction with a form element.
>
> **[3:56](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/building-great-forms-with-html-and-css/new-html5-form-tags?u=76281980&t=236)** Here we have a simple example with an input type of range being added to a numeric value.
>
> **[4:04](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/building-great-forms-with-html-and-css/new-html5-form-tags?u=76281980&t=244)** The results are going to be displayed inside of the output tag.
>
> **[4:09](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/building-great-forms-with-html-and-css/new-html5-form-tags?u=76281980&t=249)** So I'll go ahead and I'll create my output tag, and I'm going to have to assign some attributes to the output tag, the first one is going to be name field, and I'm going to give this a value of result, and then I'm going to assign an attribute of a for field, and the for field is going to pass in values of X and Y.
>
> **[4:33](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/building-great-forms-with-html-and-css/new-html5-form-tags?u=76281980&t=273)** The results of the user selecting some numeric number from a range slider and then adding it to the value of 10 in the input field that follows are going to be calculated and displayed within the output field.
>
> **[4:48](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/building-great-forms-with-html-and-css/new-html5-form-tags?u=76281980&t=288)** We're using a little bit of JavaScript to be able to complete this.
>
> **[4:53](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/building-great-forms-with-html-and-css/new-html5-form-tags?u=76281980&t=293)** So you can see that here, I'm using one of the HTML5 form events.
>
> **[4:58](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/building-great-forms-with-html-and-css/new-html5-form-tags?u=76281980&t=298)** These events occur due with the user interacting with the HTML form controls.
>
> **[5:03](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/building-great-forms-with-html-and-css/new-html5-form-tags?u=76281980&t=303)** Oninput is going to fire when the value of an element is changed by the user.
>
> **[5:09](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/building-great-forms-with-html-and-css/new-html5-form-tags?u=76281980&t=309)** If we go ahead and save this and open it in the browser, you'll see that here I have my slider, here I have my default value of 10, and if I increase the slider, you can see that the number is going to increase.
>
> **[5:24](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/building-great-forms-with-html-and-css/new-html5-form-tags?u=76281980&t=324)** So, the range slider is going to give us a value of zero to 100.
>
> **[5:29](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/building-great-forms-with-html-and-css/new-html5-form-tags?u=76281980&t=329)** We also can increase the value in the input field, and therefore increase the output calculation.
>
> **[5:36](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/building-great-forms-with-html-and-css/new-html5-form-tags?u=76281980&t=336)** These two form elements may be a little specific, but they can be useful in the right scenario.
>
> **[5:42](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/building-great-forms-with-html-and-css/new-html5-form-tags?u=76281980&t=342)** We will get more mileage with all of the new input types that are introduced in HTML5.
>
> **[5:47](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/building-great-forms-with-html-and-css/new-html5-form-tags?u=76281980&t=347)** We've already been looking at a few of these here in this example, we're using the type of range and the type of number, but we're going to dive in and look at these more in detail coming up next.

#### New input attributes
> [LinkedIn Learning](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/building-great-forms-with-html-and-css/new-input-attributes?u=76281980)

> [!transcript]- Transcript
>
> **[0:00](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/building-great-forms-with-html-and-css/new-input-attributes?u=76281980&t=0)** - [Instructor] In addition to setting the type attribute on an input field, we have a plethora of additional attributes that can make our forms more user friendly.
>
> **[0:09](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/building-great-forms-with-html-and-css/new-input-attributes?u=76281980&t=9)** Now we've already discussed the necessity of setting a name attribute.
>
> **[0:14](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/building-great-forms-with-html-and-css/new-input-attributes?u=76281980&t=14)** This is used to reference elements in a JavaScript file, or to reference form data after the form has been submitted.
>
> **[0:21](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/building-great-forms-with-html-and-css/new-input-attributes?u=76281980&t=21)** As well as the ID attribute, this associates the input with its labels for attribute and can be used with CSS and JavaScript.
>
> **[0:31](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/building-great-forms-with-html-and-css/new-input-attributes?u=76281980&t=31)** Remember that each ID needs to be unique per page.
>
> **[0:36](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/building-great-forms-with-html-and-css/new-input-attributes?u=76281980&t=36)** In addition to these attributes, we have some other helpful ones.
>
> **[0:40](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/building-great-forms-with-html-and-css/new-input-attributes?u=76281980&t=40)** The first one that we're going to be looking at is the value attribute.
>
> **[0:44](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/building-great-forms-with-html-and-css/new-input-attributes?u=76281980&t=44)** This is going to specify the initial value for an input field.
>
> **[0:48](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/building-great-forms-with-html-and-css/new-input-attributes?u=76281980&t=48)** Setting the value can be problematic, since users may overlook the field thinking it has already been filled out, or it could cause issues with validation.
>
> **[0:57](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/building-great-forms-with-html-and-css/new-input-attributes?u=76281980&t=57)** So be careful using this.
>
> **[0:59](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/building-great-forms-with-html-and-css/new-input-attributes?u=76281980&t=59)** When you set the value, the value can be changed, it just isn't super user friendly to have this already filled out.
>
> **[1:08](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/building-great-forms-with-html-and-css/new-input-attributes?u=76281980&t=68)** If you want to set something that gives the user an idea of what should go into the field, you're better off using the placeholder attribute.
>
> **[1:17](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/building-great-forms-with-html-and-css/new-input-attributes?u=76281980&t=77)** The placeholder attribute is used to specify text that will appear within the form.
>
> **[1:25](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/building-great-forms-with-html-and-css/new-input-attributes?u=76281980&t=85)** Usually this text should be a short hint that describes the expected value of an input field or text area.
>
> **[1:33](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/building-great-forms-with-html-and-css/new-input-attributes?u=76281980&t=93)** The short hint is displayed in the field before the user enters a value.
>
> **[1:38](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/building-great-forms-with-html-and-css/new-input-attributes?u=76281980&t=98)** As soon as the user starts to type, the placeholder information will disappear.
>
> **[1:44](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/building-great-forms-with-html-and-css/new-input-attributes?u=76281980&t=104)** There are some usability concerns with placeholder, so make sure when you use it, you're aware of those and are doing so in a meaningful manner.
>
> **[1:53](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/building-great-forms-with-html-and-css/new-input-attributes?u=76281980&t=113)** The next field that we're going to be looking at is the readonly field.
>
> **[1:57](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/building-great-forms-with-html-and-css/new-input-attributes?u=76281980&t=117)** Readonly is a Boolean that gets passed into the input field.
>
> **[2:01](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/building-great-forms-with-html-and-css/new-input-attributes?u=76281980&t=121)** This attribute specifies that the input field is read only and cannot be changed.
>
> **[2:06](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/building-great-forms-with-html-and-css/new-input-attributes?u=76281980&t=126)** So even though I can click in this field, I can't type in the field.
>
> **[2:11](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/building-great-forms-with-html-and-css/new-input-attributes?u=76281980&t=131)** We also have a disabled attribute, which again, is a Boolean.
>
> **[2:15](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/building-great-forms-with-html-and-css/new-input-attributes?u=76281980&t=135)** This attribute is going to specify that the input field is disabled.
>
> **[2:20](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/building-great-forms-with-html-and-css/new-input-attributes?u=76281980&t=140)** A disabled input field is unusable and unclickable, and its value will not be sent when submitting the form.
>
> **[2:28](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/building-great-forms-with-html-and-css/new-input-attributes?u=76281980&t=148)** We also have a size attribute.
>
> **[2:30](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/building-great-forms-with-html-and-css/new-input-attributes?u=76281980&t=150)** This is used to specify the visible width, in terms of characters, of an input element.
>
> **[2:37](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/building-great-forms-with-html-and-css/new-input-attributes?u=76281980&t=157)** It works with many of the input fields, such as text, search, tell, URL, email, and password.
>
> **[2:46](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/building-great-forms-with-html-and-css/new-input-attributes?u=76281980&t=166)** It represents the number of characters that you will see.
>
> **[2:50](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/building-great-forms-with-html-and-css/new-input-attributes?u=76281980&t=170)** I generally prefer to style this with CSS, but in a pinch it does work.
>
> **[2:56](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/building-great-forms-with-html-and-css/new-input-attributes?u=76281980&t=176)** As you can see, I've actually entered more than 10 characters in this field, but visibly, we can only see the first 10 or so characters.
>
> **[3:07](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/building-great-forms-with-html-and-css/new-input-attributes?u=76281980&t=187)** The maxlength attribute specifies the maximum allowed length for an input field.
>
> **[3:14](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/building-great-forms-with-html-and-css/new-input-attributes?u=76281980&t=194)** Not the visible length, as shown with the size field, but this is going to restrict the number of characters that can be entered into that field.
>
> **[3:24](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/building-great-forms-with-html-and-css/new-input-attributes?u=76281980&t=204)** The maxlength attribute does not provide any sort of feedback to the user, so if they continue to try to enter information, they may be confused as to why they can't.
>
> **[3:33](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/building-great-forms-with-html-and-css/new-input-attributes?u=76281980&t=213)** You will need to alert your user, or somehow specify, that they can only enter a certain amount of characters.
>
> **[3:41](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/building-great-forms-with-html-and-css/new-input-attributes?u=76281980&t=221)** You could do this by adding a helper tip, or by using some JavaScript code.
>
> **[3:46](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/building-great-forms-with-html-and-css/new-input-attributes?u=76281980&t=226)** All of these attributes have been around for some time, they are useful in certain situations, and I did want to make you aware of them.

#### More input attributes
> [LinkedIn Learning](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/building-great-forms-with-html-and-css/more-input-attributes?u=76281980)

> [!transcript]- Transcript
>
> **[0:00](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/building-great-forms-with-html-and-css/more-input-attributes?u=76281980&t=0)** - [Instructor] As of HTML5, we have some additional attributes that were added to the spec.
>
> **[0:05](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/building-great-forms-with-html-and-css/more-input-attributes?u=76281980&t=5)** Let's look at some of those.
>
> **[0:07](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/building-great-forms-with-html-and-css/more-input-attributes?u=76281980&t=7)** The first one that we're going to be looking at is autofocus.
>
> **[0:11](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/building-great-forms-with-html-and-css/more-input-attributes?u=76281980&t=11)** The autofocus attribute is going to specify that the input field should automatically get focus when the page loads.
>
> **[0:19](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/building-great-forms-with-html-and-css/more-input-attributes?u=76281980&t=19)** Autofocus is a Boolean, which means if it's present, it is going to be turned on or working.
>
> **[0:25](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/building-great-forms-with-html-and-css/more-input-attributes?u=76281980&t=25)** And if it isn't present, that feature is not going to be functioning in the same way.
>
> **[0:31](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/building-great-forms-with-html-and-css/more-input-attributes?u=76281980&t=31)** You can only use autofocus on one element per page.
>
> **[0:35](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/building-great-forms-with-html-and-css/more-input-attributes?u=76281980&t=35)** In addition to highlighting that particular field with the focus styles, the blinking insertion point is already ready for us to enter something into that field.
>
> **[0:48](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/building-great-forms-with-html-and-css/more-input-attributes?u=76281980&t=48)** The required attribute probably doesn't need much of an introduction.
>
> **[0:52](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/building-great-forms-with-html-and-css/more-input-attributes?u=76281980&t=52)** Like autofocus, it does exactly what you expect it to do.
>
> **[0:56](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/building-great-forms-with-html-and-css/more-input-attributes?u=76281980&t=56)** By adding it to a form field, the browser is going to require that the user enter data into that field before submitting the field.
>
> **[1:05](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/building-great-forms-with-html-and-css/more-input-attributes?u=76281980&t=65)** This replaces the basic form validation, currently implemented with JavaScript, making things a little more usable and saving us a little more development time.
>
> **[1:15](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/building-great-forms-with-html-and-css/more-input-attributes?u=76281980&t=75)** You can also style the input using the required pseudo class.
>
> **[1:19](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/building-great-forms-with-html-and-css/more-input-attributes?u=76281980&t=79)** And we'll look at that a little bit later.
>
> **[1:21](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/building-great-forms-with-html-and-css/more-input-attributes?u=76281980&t=81)** I'm using the required on this input field that is a type of text and I also have the required attribute on an input field that is a type of email.
>
> **[1:31](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/building-great-forms-with-html-and-css/more-input-attributes?u=76281980&t=91)** If I go back to my form and try to submit, I'm going to get the message that tells me to fill out this field.
>
> **[1:38](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/building-great-forms-with-html-and-css/more-input-attributes?u=76281980&t=98)** If I fill out this field, but don't do so correctly, then I'm going to get another helper tip, which is going to let me know that I need to add the add symbol and possibly some other information.
>
> **[1:51](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/building-great-forms-with-html-and-css/more-input-attributes?u=76281980&t=111)** It will keep doing that until I've added information that is acceptable for that field.
>
> **[1:57](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/building-great-forms-with-html-and-css/more-input-attributes?u=76281980&t=117)** This is not testing the validity of this particular field, but only that it's following the pattern that a type of email is expected to have.
>
> **[2:08](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/building-great-forms-with-html-and-css/more-input-attributes?u=76281980&t=128)** The next attribute that we're going to be looking at is the datalist element, which is used along with the list attribute.
>
> **[2:17](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/building-great-forms-with-html-and-css/more-input-attributes?u=76281980&t=137)** The datalist element is new in HTML5, and it represents a predefined list of options for form controls.
>
> **[2:25](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/building-great-forms-with-html-and-css/more-input-attributes?u=76281980&t=145)** It works in a similar way to the in browser search boxes that auto complete as you type, the list attribute is going to enable the user to associate a list of options with a particular field.
>
> **[2:39](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/building-great-forms-with-html-and-css/more-input-attributes?u=76281980&t=159)** The value of the list attribute must be the same as the ID on the datalist element.
>
> **[2:48](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/building-great-forms-with-html-and-css/more-input-attributes?u=76281980&t=168)** They need to also reside within the same document.
>
> **[2:51](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/building-great-forms-with-html-and-css/more-input-attributes?u=76281980&t=171)** It is worth noting that the datalist element is not entirely supported across the board.
>
> **[2:57](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/building-great-forms-with-html-and-css/more-input-attributes?u=76281980&t=177)** If we go to the Can I use website, and search of datalist, you're going to see that many of the modern browsers have partial support for this particular element.
>
> **[3:09](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/building-great-forms-with-html-and-css/more-input-attributes?u=76281980&t=189)** There are quite a few of the browsers however, that do fully support this.
>
> **[3:14](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/building-great-forms-with-html-and-css/more-input-attributes?u=76281980&t=194)** But if you are going to use it, I would encourage you to research and make sure that your user base is going to be able to utilize this particular feature.
>
> **[3:23](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/building-great-forms-with-html-and-css/more-input-attributes?u=76281980&t=203)** In addition to selecting something from the pre determined list, you can also enter your own information within the list.
>
> **[3:32](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/building-great-forms-with-html-and-css/more-input-attributes?u=76281980&t=212)** So this is like a list drop down menu on steroids.
>
> **[3:36](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/building-great-forms-with-html-and-css/more-input-attributes?u=76281980&t=216)** The next field that we're going to look at is the multiple field.
>
> **[3:40](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/building-great-forms-with-html-and-css/more-input-attributes?u=76281980&t=220)** Now I have two fields for email.
>
> **[3:44](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/building-great-forms-with-html-and-css/more-input-attributes?u=76281980&t=224)** The first one does not contain the multiple attribute.
>
> **[3:47](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/building-great-forms-with-html-and-css/more-input-attributes?u=76281980&t=227)** The second one does contain the multiple attribute.
>
> **[3:51](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/building-great-forms-with-html-and-css/more-input-attributes?u=76281980&t=231)** So if I go to the first field and enter an email address, and then put a comma and try to enter another email address and click Submit, I'm going to get an error message.
>
> **[4:03](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/building-great-forms-with-html-and-css/more-input-attributes?u=76281980&t=243)** It does not accept the comma or anything that follows that.
>
> **[4:08](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/building-great-forms-with-html-and-css/more-input-attributes?u=76281980&t=248)** If you do, however, set the multiple attribute on the field, then you can add multiple items, they simply need to be separated by a comma and the field will accept that.
>
> **[4:19](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/building-great-forms-with-html-and-css/more-input-attributes?u=76281980&t=259)** In addition to adding multiple on the email types, I've also set an input type to file with the attribute of multiple.
>
> **[4:29](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/building-great-forms-with-html-and-css/more-input-attributes?u=76281980&t=269)** The file input type is going to let the user choose one or more files from their device and upload it to the server for submission.
>
> **[4:38](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/building-great-forms-with-html-and-css/more-input-attributes?u=76281980&t=278)** So when we add multiple, the user could add more than one file.
>
> **[4:42](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/building-great-forms-with-html-and-css/more-input-attributes?u=76281980&t=282)** The final attributes that we're going to be looking at are the min, max, and step attributes.
>
> **[4:48](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/building-great-forms-with-html-and-css/more-input-attributes?u=76281980&t=288)** The input field will need to have a type set of number range or one of the date time values in order for this to work.
>
> **[4:56](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/building-great-forms-with-html-and-css/more-input-attributes?u=76281980&t=296)** In the case of using a numeric number type in most browsers when this field is in focus, you will see two small arrows that are going to allow you to increase or decrease the value.
>
> **[5:09](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/building-great-forms-with-html-and-css/more-input-attributes?u=76281980&t=309)** Now as you can see, as I increase the values on this particular field, I am stepping by twos.
>
> **[5:16](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/building-great-forms-with-html-and-css/more-input-attributes?u=76281980&t=316)** The reason that this is happening is because I've added the step attribute and set it to two, which means that this attribute is going to count by two.
>
> **[5:26](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/building-great-forms-with-html-and-css/more-input-attributes?u=76281980&t=326)** I've also set the step attribute on my range slider.
>
> **[5:30](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/building-great-forms-with-html-and-css/more-input-attributes?u=76281980&t=330)** My range slider has a minimum amount of zero and the maximum amount of 80 with a step of five.
>
> **[5:36](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/building-great-forms-with-html-and-css/more-input-attributes?u=76281980&t=336)** If I use the slider, you can see that my values are going to be increasing by multiples of five, and they will only go to 80.
>
> **[5:47](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/building-great-forms-with-html-and-css/more-input-attributes?u=76281980&t=347)** Of course, I'm adding 10, so I'll get a result of 90.
>
> **[5:50](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/building-great-forms-with-html-and-css/more-input-attributes?u=76281980&t=350)** I've gone ahead and specified that this range slider can go no higher than 80 and the minimum is set to zero.
>
> **[5:59](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/building-great-forms-with-html-and-css/more-input-attributes?u=76281980&t=359)** Using min, max, and step attributes will enable you to build all sorts of sophisticated elements.
>
> **[6:06](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/building-great-forms-with-html-and-css/more-input-attributes?u=76281980&t=366)** It is worth noting that on this particular slider, I did have to add the attribute of on input and set it to a value of some JavaScript so that when the user is sliding, the range slider, my output element is going to go ahead and display the results of the range slider and my input type of number.
>
> **[6:32](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/building-great-forms-with-html-and-css/more-input-attributes?u=76281980&t=392)** So by themselves, these do not have this behavior.
>
> **[6:35](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/building-great-forms-with-html-and-css/more-input-attributes?u=76281980&t=395)** We need to incorporate a little bit of JavaScript for this to work as well.
>
> **[6:39](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/building-great-forms-with-html-and-css/more-input-attributes?u=76281980&t=399)** So now we've looked at many of the new form attributes that help improve user experience and save you development time.
>
> **[6:47](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/building-great-forms-with-html-and-css/more-input-attributes?u=76281980&t=407)** I think that you'll find the opportunity to incorporate many of these into the forms that you're going to be building.

#### Input restrictions: Pattern
> [LinkedIn Learning](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/building-great-forms-with-html-and-css/input-restrictions-pattern?u=76281980)

> [!transcript]- Transcript
>
> **[0:00](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/building-great-forms-with-html-and-css/input-restrictions-pattern?u=76281980&t=0)** - [Instructor] As we have seen, the HTML5 specification has made validation that much easier with the introduction of the new input types, such as email, URL and tel.
>
> **[0:10](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/building-great-forms-with-html-and-css/input-restrictions-pattern?u=76281980&t=10)** And these also come packaged up with predefined validation.
>
> **[0:14](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/building-great-forms-with-html-and-css/input-restrictions-pattern?u=76281980&t=14)** Whenever the value given is not met with the expected formatting , these input types will throw an error message, thus preventing submission.
>
> **[0:23](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/building-great-forms-with-html-and-css/input-restrictions-pattern?u=76281980&t=23)** However, expecting every possible input scenario to be catered for is impractical.
>
> **[0:28](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/building-great-forms-with-html-and-css/input-restrictions-pattern?u=76281980&t=28)** What if you have a username "zipcode", "credit card" or any other special data types that are not specified as standard input types?
>
> **[0:36](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/building-great-forms-with-html-and-css/input-restrictions-pattern?u=76281980&t=36)** How do we validate those inputs?
>
> **[0:38](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/building-great-forms-with-html-and-css/input-restrictions-pattern?u=76281980&t=38)** This is where the attribute pattern comes into play.
>
> **[0:42](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/building-great-forms-with-html-and-css/input-restrictions-pattern?u=76281980&t=42)** The pattern attribute is only applicable on the input element.
>
> **[0:46](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/building-great-forms-with-html-and-css/input-restrictions-pattern?u=76281980&t=46)** It allows us to define our own rule to validate the input value using regular expressions or regex.
>
> **[0:54](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/building-great-forms-with-html-and-css/input-restrictions-pattern?u=76281980&t=54)** Again, if the value does not match the specified pattern, the input will throw an error.
>
> **[1:00](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/building-great-forms-with-html-and-css/input-restrictions-pattern?u=76281980&t=60)** Regular expressions, or regex for short, is extremely powerful in searching and manipulating text strings, particularly in processing text files.
>
> **[1:10](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/building-great-forms-with-html-and-css/input-restrictions-pattern?u=76281980&t=70)** One line of regex can easily replace several dozen lines of programming code.
>
> **[1:16](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/building-great-forms-with-html-and-css/input-restrictions-pattern?u=76281980&t=76)** Here's a code snippet that is asking our end user for a username.
>
> **[1:20](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/building-great-forms-with-html-and-css/input-restrictions-pattern?u=76281980&t=80)** As you can see, I'm using the pattern attribute.
>
> **[1:23](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/building-great-forms-with-html-and-css/input-restrictions-pattern?u=76281980&t=83)** Inside of the square braces, we pass on our conditions.
>
> **[1:27](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/building-great-forms-with-html-and-css/input-restrictions-pattern?u=76281980&t=87)** Here I'm saying uppercase A through Z is okay.

#### Buttons
> [LinkedIn Learning](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/building-great-forms-with-html-and-css/buttons?u=76281980)

> **[0:00](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/building-great-forms-with-html-and-css/buttons?u=76281980&t=0)** - [Instructor] A buttons is an interactive element that results in an action that is described on it.
>
> **[0:06](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/building-great-forms-with-html-and-css/buttons?u=76281980&t=6)** The HTML button element represents a clickable button, which can be used in forms or anywhere in a document that needs simple standard button functionality.
>
> **[0:16](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/building-great-forms-with-html-and-css/buttons?u=76281980&t=16)** By default, HTML buttons are typically presented in a similar style to that of the host platform that the user agent is running on.
>
> **[0:26](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/building-great-forms-with-html-and-css/buttons?u=76281980&t=26)** Basically, meaning that the browser has control of how the buttons may look.

> **[2:12](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/building-great-forms-with-html-and-css/buttons?u=76281980&t=132)** This is just going to make a generic button.
>
> **[2:15](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/building-great-forms-with-html-and-css/buttons?u=76281980&t=135)** We also have a button with a type of button.
>
> **[2:19](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/building-great-forms-with-html-and-css/buttons?u=76281980&t=139)** The default behavior of the button is button.
>
> **[2:23](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/building-great-forms-with-html-and-css/buttons?u=76281980&t=143)** In addition to that, we can supply the type of submit, which is going to go ahead and submit the current form data.
>
> **[2:32](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/building-great-forms-with-html-and-css/buttons?u=76281980&t=152)** This is the default behavior.
>
> **[2:34](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/building-great-forms-with-html-and-css/buttons?u=76281980&t=154)** We could also add a type of reset, which will reset the data in the current form.
>
> **[2:39](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/building-great-forms-with-html-and-css/buttons?u=76281980&t=159)** Or we could just have the type be set to a button.
>
> **[2:42](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/building-great-forms-with-html-and-css/buttons?u=76281980&t=162)** If it's set to a button, its effects must be controlled by something else such as JavaScript.
>
> **[2:48](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/building-great-forms-with-html-and-css/buttons?u=76281980&t=168)** Buttons have no default behavior and they do nothing when pressed.
>
> **[2:52](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/building-great-forms-with-html-and-css/buttons?u=76281980&t=172)** You can have client side scripts, associated with an elements events and they're going to be triggered when the event occurs.
>
> **[3:00](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/building-great-forms-with-html-and-css/buttons?u=76281980&t=180)** The value attribute of the input tag is going to control what text is displayed on the button.
>
> **[3:11](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/building-great-forms-with-html-and-css/buttons?u=76281980&t=191)** The value is also passed to the server when the form is submitted.
>
> **[3:16](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/building-great-forms-with-html-and-css/buttons?u=76281980&t=196)** Unlike the input tag, a button tag has an opening and closing tag.
>
> **[3:21](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/building-great-forms-with-html-and-css/buttons?u=76281980&t=201)** The text that appears on the button is going to be contained as a text node in between the opening and closing tags.
>
> **[3:29](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/building-great-forms-with-html-and-css/buttons?u=76281980&t=209)** Now what we're going to do is we're going to customize the look of our buttons, by adding some CSS.
>
> **[3:35](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/building-great-forms-with-html-and-css/buttons?u=76281980&t=215)** Button elements are much easier to style, rather than the input elements.
>
> **[3:40](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/building-great-forms-with-html-and-css/buttons?u=76281980&t=220)** You can add inner HTML content, think of things like m, strong or even images, or you can use the pseudo classes of after and before to achieve complex rendering, while input only accepts a textual value attribute, let's go ahead and let's customize these buttons down at the bottom of the page.
>
> **[4:01](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/building-great-forms-with-html-and-css/buttons?u=76281980&t=241)** My first button has an ID of Check button.
>
> **[4:04](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/building-great-forms-with-html-and-css/buttons?u=76281980&t=244)** And the second one has an ID of Apply button.
>
> **[4:07](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/building-great-forms-with-html-and-css/buttons?u=76281980&t=247)** I'm going to go into my code snippet area, and I'm going to copy the CSS code that resides under step two.
>
> **[4:15](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/building-great-forms-with-html-and-css/buttons?u=76281980&t=255)** I'll go back into my CSS file, and we'll paste this at the bottom of our page.
>
> **[4:20](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/building-great-forms-with-html-and-css/buttons?u=76281980&t=260)** Let me just take a minute to explain this code.
>
> **[4:23](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/building-great-forms-with-html-and-css/buttons?u=76281980&t=263)** Once I show you what it looks like in the browser.
>
> **[4:25](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/building-great-forms-with-html-and-css/buttons?u=76281980&t=265)** I'll save my page and I'll refresh in the browser.
>
> **[4:28](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/building-great-forms-with-html-and-css/buttons?u=76281980&t=268)** As you can see, these buttons are much more interesting and much more dynamic than the buttons that we were displaying before.
>
> **[4:36](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/building-great-forms-with-html-and-css/buttons?u=76281980&t=276)** These buttons are very similar in how I am styling them.
>
> **[4:40](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/building-great-forms-with-html-and-css/buttons?u=76281980&t=280)** They are for the most part going to contain a background color, which is fallback content in case the browser does not support a linear gradient.
>
> **[4:49](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/building-great-forms-with-html-and-css/buttons?u=76281980&t=289)** Then we have the linear gradient code.
>
> **[4:51](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/building-great-forms-with-html-and-css/buttons?u=76281980&t=291)** I'm setting the text color to white.
>
> **[4:54](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/building-great-forms-with-html-and-css/buttons?u=76281980&t=294)** I'm transforming my text in the Check button to be uppercase specifying font size, padding and border radius.
>
> **[5:03](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/building-great-forms-with-html-and-css/buttons?u=76281980&t=303)** I'm also using the pseudo class of before.
>
> **[5:06](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/building-great-forms-with-html-and-css/buttons?u=76281980&t=306)** This is how I'm adding the little icon to my button.
>
> **[5:10](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/building-great-forms-with-html-and-css/buttons?u=76281980&t=310)** So I am using Font Awesome in order to add the icon, and I need to specify the font family, the font weight.
>
> **[5:18](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/building-great-forms-with-html-and-css/buttons?u=76281980&t=318)** This unit code is the code that is going to align with the checkout icon and some padding on the right.
>
> **[5:27](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/building-great-forms-with-html-and-css/buttons?u=76281980&t=327)** The Apply button is set up very similarly to the Check button.
>
> **[5:31](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/building-great-forms-with-html-and-css/buttons?u=76281980&t=331)** As you can see from looking in here, I've just changed some of the things in regards to the color of the gradient.
>
> **[5:38](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/building-great-forms-with-html-and-css/buttons?u=76281980&t=338)** I've added a box shadow and I'm using a different Font Awesome icon and I have it in a different location.
>
> **[5:45](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/building-great-forms-with-html-and-css/buttons?u=76281980&t=345)** I'm using the pseudo class of after for the Apply button.
>
> **[5:49](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/building-great-forms-with-html-and-css/buttons?u=76281980&t=349)** So that the icon appears after the tags instead of before the tags as it does with a Checkout button.
>
> **[5:56](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/building-great-forms-with-html-and-css/buttons?u=76281980&t=356)** If your buttons are not for submitting form data to a server Be sure to set their type attribute to button.
>
> **[6:03](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/building-great-forms-with-html-and-css/buttons?u=76281980&t=363)** Otherwise, they will try to submit the form data and load the non existent response, possibly destroying the current state of the document.
>
> **[6:13](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/building-great-forms-with-html-and-css/buttons?u=76281980&t=373)** All in all, some tips for styling buttons are to make your button look like a button.
>
> **[6:18](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/building-great-forms-with-html-and-css/buttons?u=76281980&t=378)** Make buttons the right minimum size.
>
> **[6:21](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/building-great-forms-with-html-and-css/buttons?u=76281980&t=381)** If a button is too small, it'll be difficult to click, it should be at least 44 by 44 pixels to accommodate fingers.
>
> **[6:30](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/building-great-forms-with-html-and-css/buttons?u=76281980&t=390)** Important buttons also work well with icons.
>
> **[6:33](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/building-great-forms-with-html-and-css/buttons?u=76281980&t=393)** A checkout is quickly identified by a basket or a cart icon, but only as long as the word checkout also appears.
>
> **[6:41](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/building-great-forms-with-html-and-css/buttons?u=76281980&t=401)** A right arrow or a chevron placed after the button label makes the resulting message stronger.
>
> **[6:47](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/building-great-forms-with-html-and-css/buttons?u=76281980&t=407)** The user is more compelled to click and proceed.
>
> **[6:50](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/building-great-forms-with-html-and-css/buttons?u=76281980&t=410)** This works well if you'd like to strengthen your call to action.
>
> **[6:54](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/building-great-forms-with-html-and-css/buttons?u=76281980&t=414)** Buttons with shadows are also more clickable and noticed much faster than flat ones.
>
> **[7:00](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/building-great-forms-with-html-and-css/buttons?u=76281980&t=420)** And rounded buttons are considered friendlier and more positive than sharp edges.
>
> **[7:05](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/building-great-forms-with-html-and-css/buttons?u=76281980&t=425)** Now that you know a little bit about buttons, have fun styling your own custom buttons so they accommodate the look and feel of the website that your form exists in.
