#### Autocomplete
> [LinkedIn Learning](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/building-great-forms-with-html-and-css/autocomplete?u=76281980)

> [!transcript]- Transcript
>
> **[0:00](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/building-great-forms-with-html-and-css/autocomplete?u=76281980&t=0)** - [Instructor] The HTML Autocomplete attribute is available on the form tag as well as input elements that take a text or numeric value as input, text area elements and select elements.
>
> **[0:12](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/building-great-forms-with-html-and-css/autocomplete?u=76281980&t=12)** Autocomplete lets web developers specify what if any permissions the user agent has to provide automated assistance in filling out form field values as well as guidance to the browser as to what type of information is expected in that particular field.
>
> **[0:29](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/building-great-forms-with-html-and-css/autocomplete?u=76281980&t=29)** The source of the suggested values is generally up to the browser.
>
> **[0:33](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/building-great-forms-with-html-and-css/autocomplete?u=76281980&t=33)** Typically values come from past values entered by the user, but they may also come from pre-configured values.
>
> **[0:41](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/building-great-forms-with-html-and-css/autocomplete?u=76281980&t=41)** For instance a browser might let the user save their name, address, phone number and email addresses for Autocomplete purposes.
>
> **[0:49](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/building-great-forms-with-html-and-css/autocomplete?u=76281980&t=49)** Perhaps the browser offers the ability to save encrypted credit card information for auto completion following an authentication procedure.
>
> **[0:57](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/building-great-forms-with-html-and-css/autocomplete?u=76281980&t=57)** The auto complete attribute specifies whether input elements can by default have their values automatically completed by the browser or not.
>
> **[1:06](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/building-great-forms-with-html-and-css/autocomplete?u=76281980&t=66)** In the example here, I have the first form field, and I'm going ahead and setting the Autocomplete attribute for the email input as well as for the telephone input.
>
> **[1:22](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/building-great-forms-with-html-and-css/autocomplete?u=76281980&t=82)** So if we jump into the browser, and we try to enter some information into this field, the browser is going to show me any other emails that have been entered previously.
>
> **[1:35](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/building-great-forms-with-html-and-css/autocomplete?u=76281980&t=95)** It remembers that information, and I can quickly populate my form with that information.
>
> **[1:41](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/building-great-forms-with-html-and-css/autocomplete?u=76281980&t=101)** If we compare that to the next form field, which is right here, and you can see that I have Autocomplete set to off, when we go ahead and try to click in these fields, I have no suggestion.
>
> **[1:59](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/building-great-forms-with-html-and-css/autocomplete?u=76281980&t=119)** It's not remembering any of the information that I had previously entered into my form.
>
> **[2:06](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/building-great-forms-with-html-and-css/autocomplete?u=76281980&t=126)** So when we have Autocomplete set to off, the browser may not automatically complete entries.
>
> **[2:12](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/building-great-forms-with-html-and-css/autocomplete?u=76281980&t=132)** When we have the Autocomplete specified for a specific field, or on the entire form, if we set Autocomplete to on, then the browser's going to automatically complete values based on values that the user has entered before.
>
> **[2:31](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/building-great-forms-with-html-and-css/autocomplete?u=76281980&t=151)** The default state is set to on.
>
> **[2:33](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/building-great-forms-with-html-and-css/autocomplete?u=76281980&t=153)** This means that generally, we won't have to use it unless we specifically want to turn it off.
>
> **[2:39](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/building-great-forms-with-html-and-css/autocomplete?u=76281980&t=159)** The last example I have for you in this section is an example where we have Autocomplete turned on and off on individual fields, so on this form, you can see that I have Autocomplete set for the email, but I have it turned off for the telephone number, and if we come into the browser, when I click in the email field, it's remembering a previously entered email address and entering it if I desire, and in the phone field, it doesn't remember anything since we have Autocomplete set to off.
>
> **[3:14](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/building-great-forms-with-html-and-css/autocomplete?u=76281980&t=194)** The Autocomplete attribute helps users complete forms based on earlier inputs.
>
> **[3:19](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/building-great-forms-with-html-and-css/autocomplete?u=76281980&t=199)** In certain cases, you may want to restrict this behavior by using Autocomplete off so that the browser is not able to implement this information.

### 3. Form-Specific CSS

#### Form layout tips
> [LinkedIn Learning](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/building-great-forms-with-html-and-css/form-layout-tips?u=76281980)

> [!transcript]- Transcript
>
> **[0:00](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/building-great-forms-with-html-and-css/form-layout-tips?u=76281980&t=0)** - [Instructor] Form layout is a crucial element of form design that many designers and website managers neglect and often leave to the last stage of development.
>
> **[0:09](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/building-great-forms-with-html-and-css/form-layout-tips?u=76281980&t=9)** Since the form is the key method in which you'll be interacting with your users, you want to consider and plan for the physical structure of the form prior to building it.
>
> **[0:19](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/building-great-forms-with-html-and-css/form-layout-tips?u=76281980&t=19)** Some things for you to consider are, don't duplicate paper forms.
>
> **[0:24](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/building-great-forms-with-html-and-css/form-layout-tips?u=76281980&t=24)** Web Forms don't have to follow the same conventions as paper forms.
>
> **[0:28](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/building-great-forms-with-html-and-css/form-layout-tips?u=76281980&t=28)** People use paper forms and web forms differently.
>
> **[0:31](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/building-great-forms-with-html-and-css/form-layout-tips?u=76281980&t=31)** If you simply convert a paper form, without any changes, you'll probably reduce your conversions.
>
> **[0:37](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/building-great-forms-with-html-and-css/form-layout-tips?u=76281980&t=37)** When you design a paper form, you're constrained by the format, by the space.
>
> **[0:42](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/building-great-forms-with-html-and-css/form-layout-tips?u=76281980&t=42)** You have to fit everything on that one page.
>
> **[0:45](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/building-great-forms-with-html-and-css/form-layout-tips?u=76281980&t=45)** The result is usually a cramped cluttered mess that fits but doesn't consider usability.
>
> **[0:51](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/building-great-forms-with-html-and-css/form-layout-tips?u=76281980&t=51)** When you design web forms, don't be afraid to use space.
>
> **[0:55](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/building-great-forms-with-html-and-css/form-layout-tips?u=76281980&t=55)** Build whitespace between fields, use separators, background colors, conditional logic and multiple pages to create logical divisions, and comfortable usability.
>
> **[1:06](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/building-great-forms-with-html-and-css/form-layout-tips?u=76281980&t=66)** Remember, that you're not constrained by paper, so you no longer have to save space by cramming as many fields as possible into that one little area.
>
> **[1:15](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/building-great-forms-with-html-and-css/form-layout-tips?u=76281980&t=75)** Place your form inside its own container and separate it from the rest of the content.
>
> **[1:20](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/building-great-forms-with-html-and-css/form-layout-tips?u=76281980&t=80)** Keep it nearby, but use borders, colors and positioning so users know what's part of the form and what isn't.
>
> **[1:28](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/building-great-forms-with-html-and-css/form-layout-tips?u=76281980&t=88)** When possible, reduce mouse usage.
>
> **[1:31](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/building-great-forms-with-html-and-css/form-layout-tips?u=76281980&t=91)** Forms should be easy to complete.
>
> **[1:33](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/building-great-forms-with-html-and-css/form-layout-tips?u=76281980&t=93)** Your users should be able to move through the form seamlessly and intuitively without using their mouse.
>
> **[1:39](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/building-great-forms-with-html-and-css/form-layout-tips?u=76281980&t=99)** Use each form yourself before you publish it on a page.
>
> **[1:43](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/building-great-forms-with-html-and-css/form-layout-tips?u=76281980&t=103)** Ensure that you can navigate it smoothly and swiftly without touching your mouse, other than setting your cursor in the first field that is.
>
> **[1:51](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/building-great-forms-with-html-and-css/form-layout-tips?u=76281980&t=111)** Considered drop down menus and radio buttons, for instance.
>
> **[1:55](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/building-great-forms-with-html-and-css/form-layout-tips?u=76281980&t=115)** If you use a drop down menu, the user has to click the field, scroll to the correct option and then click the option.
>
> **[2:02](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/building-great-forms-with-html-and-css/form-layout-tips?u=76281980&t=122)** That's three mouse motions.
>
> **[2:04](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/building-great-forms-with-html-and-css/form-layout-tips?u=76281980&t=124)** If you use the radio button instead, the user would only have to click once.
>
> **[2:08](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/building-great-forms-with-html-and-css/form-layout-tips?u=76281980&t=128)** If possible, use a method that doesn't require the user to leave the keyboard at all.
>
> **[2:13](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/building-great-forms-with-html-and-css/form-layout-tips?u=76281980&t=133)** Remove unnecessary fields.
>
> **[2:16](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/building-great-forms-with-html-and-css/form-layout-tips?u=76281980&t=136)** One of the first rules of form design is to reduce the number of fields as much as possible.
>
> **[2:21](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/building-great-forms-with-html-and-css/form-layout-tips?u=76281980&t=141)** This rule applies to form layout as well.
>
> **[2:24](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/building-great-forms-with-html-and-css/form-layout-tips?u=76281980&t=144)** Each field you add reduces the number of people who will complete the form.
>
> **[2:28](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/building-great-forms-with-html-and-css/form-layout-tips?u=76281980&t=148)** It's also important to consider the amount of effort you demand from the user to input data.
>
> **[2:34](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/building-great-forms-with-html-and-css/form-layout-tips?u=76281980&t=154)** Typing has a high cost to your users because it's time consuming and error prone, especially on mobile devices.
>
> **[2:41](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/building-great-forms-with-html-and-css/form-layout-tips?u=76281980&t=161)** You may be able to simplify the user experience with radio buttons or drop downs, even if they add a little complexity to your form layout.
>
> **[2:50](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/building-great-forms-with-html-and-css/form-layout-tips?u=76281980&t=170)** Match fields to the input.
>
> **[2:52](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/building-great-forms-with-html-and-css/form-layout-tips?u=76281980&t=172)** Create a visual connection to the input you are expecting to collect.
>
> **[2:56](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/building-great-forms-with-html-and-css/form-layout-tips?u=76281980&t=176)** Users doubt their understanding of the form label when the field longer or shorter than the expected data.
>
> **[3:03](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/building-great-forms-with-html-and-css/form-layout-tips?u=76281980&t=183)** This is especially true when users come across a field with a technical label, such as CVV, credit verification value.
>
> **[3:11](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/building-great-forms-with-html-and-css/form-layout-tips?u=76281980&t=191)** Or data that they don't normally input into forms.
>
> **[3:14](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/building-great-forms-with-html-and-css/form-layout-tips?u=76281980&t=194)** You can improve usability and tighten your form layout, by reducing your fields to the size of the data you expect.
>
> **[3:21](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/building-great-forms-with-html-and-css/form-layout-tips?u=76281980&t=201)** A CVV field would only have space for three digits.
>
> **[3:25](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/building-great-forms-with-html-and-css/form-layout-tips?u=76281980&t=205)** A zip code field would only have space for five digits.
>
> **[3:29](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/building-great-forms-with-html-and-css/form-layout-tips?u=76281980&t=209)** You may not know how long a user's address will be but it probably won't exceed 75 characters.
>
> **[3:36](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/building-great-forms-with-html-and-css/form-layout-tips?u=76281980&t=216)** Standardize your labeling.
>
> **[3:37](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/building-great-forms-with-html-and-css/form-layout-tips?u=76281980&t=217)** Users use field labels to understand what you expect them to do for each field.
>
> **[3:43](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/building-great-forms-with-html-and-css/form-layout-tips?u=76281980&t=223)** To prevent confusing your users, it's important to use the position labels the same way for every field.
>
> **[3:49](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/building-great-forms-with-html-and-css/form-layout-tips?u=76281980&t=229)** It's always best to place field labels just above the input.
>
> **[3:53](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/building-great-forms-with-html-and-css/form-layout-tips?u=76281980&t=233)** Top-aligned labels help users scan the form quickly.
>
> **[3:57](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/building-great-forms-with-html-and-css/form-layout-tips?u=76281980&t=237)** It helps them understand it.
>
> **[3:59](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/building-great-forms-with-html-and-css/form-layout-tips?u=76281980&t=239)** It also requires less horizontal space as compared to placing the labels to the left of the field.
>
> **[4:05](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/building-great-forms-with-html-and-css/form-layout-tips?u=76281980&t=245)** This means that you can make your fields longer and more easily accessible on smaller screens.
>
> **[4:11](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/building-great-forms-with-html-and-css/form-layout-tips?u=76281980&t=251)** Be careful when using placeholder text.
>
> **[4:14](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/building-great-forms-with-html-and-css/form-layout-tips?u=76281980&t=254)** Some form designers use the placeholder text, the text inside the field as a label.
>
> **[4:19](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/building-great-forms-with-html-and-css/form-layout-tips?u=76281980&t=259)** This can be problematic for accessibility and for everyone.
>
> **[4:23](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/building-great-forms-with-html-and-css/form-layout-tips?u=76281980&t=263)** Having the label or hint disappear when the user starts typing means that they may forget what you asked and cuts down on a user's understanding of what they should be doing in that field.
>
> **[4:34](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/building-great-forms-with-html-and-css/form-layout-tips?u=76281980&t=274)** A nice option to using placeholder text is using a floating label.
>
> **[4:39](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/building-great-forms-with-html-and-css/form-layout-tips?u=76281980&t=279)** A floating label will appear within the input area, and then when the user clicks in that particular input area, the floating label will float up to the top section of the form.
>
> **[4:51](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/building-great-forms-with-html-and-css/form-layout-tips?u=76281980&t=291)** I think that this is a great use of pseudo placeholder and utilizing labels that are persistent.
>
> **[5:00](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/building-great-forms-with-html-and-css/form-layout-tips?u=76281980&t=300)** Get rid of the reset button.
>
> **[5:01](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/building-great-forms-with-html-and-css/form-layout-tips?u=76281980&t=301)** Reset buttons distract users from the primary call to action, the submit button.
>
> **[5:07](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/building-great-forms-with-html-and-css/form-layout-tips?u=76281980&t=307)** They also present an opportunity for the user to accidentally clear their own form when they didn't intend to.
>
> **[5:13](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/building-great-forms-with-html-and-css/form-layout-tips?u=76281980&t=313)** Plan for errors.
>
> **[5:14](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/building-great-forms-with-html-and-css/form-layout-tips?u=76281980&t=314)** No matter how simple your forms are, some people will make mistakes.
>
> **[5:19](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/building-great-forms-with-html-and-css/form-layout-tips?u=76281980&t=319)** Which means you'll have to make them aware of their errors.
>
> **[5:22](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/building-great-forms-with-html-and-css/form-layout-tips?u=76281980&t=322)** Error messages should be graceful, actionable and clear.
>
> **[5:26](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/building-great-forms-with-html-and-css/form-layout-tips?u=76281980&t=326)** They should stand out from the rest of the form.
>
> **[5:29](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/building-great-forms-with-html-and-css/form-layout-tips?u=76281980&t=329)** Use icons, text colors, backgrounds, or other design elements to draw the users attention to the problem.
>
> **[5:37](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/building-great-forms-with-html-and-css/form-layout-tips?u=76281980&t=337)** Place error messages right next to the field with the error, rather than generically at the top or bottom of the form.
>
> **[5:44](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/building-great-forms-with-html-and-css/form-layout-tips?u=76281980&t=344)** Validate the forms in realtime so the user knows that there's an error before they hit the submit button.
>
> **[5:50](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/building-great-forms-with-html-and-css/form-layout-tips?u=76281980&t=350)** The best layout to use when building forms is single column.
>
> **[5:54](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/building-great-forms-with-html-and-css/form-layout-tips?u=76281980&t=354)** It may be tempting to use multiple columns on your forms so that the user can see the entire form without scrolling.
>
> **[6:00](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/building-great-forms-with-html-and-css/form-layout-tips?u=76281980&t=360)** But this is unnecessary anymore.
>
> **[6:02](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/building-great-forms-with-html-and-css/form-layout-tips?u=76281980&t=362)** People don't mind scrolling.
>
> **[6:04](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/building-great-forms-with-html-and-css/form-layout-tips?u=76281980&t=364)** Using forms with multiple columns can be confusing for your users.
>
> **[6:08](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/building-great-forms-with-html-and-css/form-layout-tips?u=76281980&t=368)** Some will expect to finish one column before addressing the other.
>
> **[6:12](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/building-great-forms-with-html-and-css/form-layout-tips?u=76281980&t=372)** Other users will expect to progress horizontally before dropping to the next line.
>
> **[6:17](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/building-great-forms-with-html-and-css/form-layout-tips?u=76281980&t=377)** Single column forms, however, are simple and easy to understand.
>
> **[6:21](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/building-great-forms-with-html-and-css/form-layout-tips?u=76281980&t=381)** There's only one way to go, so the users don't experience any type of confusion.
>
> **[6:26](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/building-great-forms-with-html-and-css/form-layout-tips?u=76281980&t=386)** Single column form layout is another way to reduce the mouse usage as well.
>
> **[6:31](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/building-great-forms-with-html-and-css/form-layout-tips?u=76281980&t=391)** It is worth noting that when you create a single column form, there may be certain form field elements that will fit better if they're side by side.
>
> **[6:42](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/building-great-forms-with-html-and-css/form-layout-tips?u=76281980&t=402)** And the example I'm showing here, the date and the security code are both fairly short fields.
>
> **[6:48](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/building-great-forms-with-html-and-css/form-layout-tips?u=76281980&t=408)** So having them appear together since they're related makes sense.
>
> **[6:52](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/building-great-forms-with-html-and-css/form-layout-tips?u=76281980&t=412)** You want to order your forms logically, think of a form like a conversation between you and the end user.
>
> **[6:59](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/building-great-forms-with-html-and-css/form-layout-tips?u=76281980&t=419)** Your job is to ask questions and they provide the answers.
>
> **[7:03](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/building-great-forms-with-html-and-css/form-layout-tips?u=76281980&t=423)** Just like a conversation, the questions on your form should flow naturally.
>
> **[7:08](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/building-great-forms-with-html-and-css/form-layout-tips?u=76281980&t=428)** Ask your questions according to a logical flow from the users perspective, and group them with similar questions.
>
> **[7:14](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/building-great-forms-with-html-and-css/form-layout-tips?u=76281980&t=434)** For instance, it makes sense to ask for the user's name and email address first.
>
> **[7:19](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/building-great-forms-with-html-and-css/form-layout-tips?u=76281980&t=439)** Shipping address is usually four or more fields, so ask for that data at the same time as well.
>
> **[7:25](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/building-great-forms-with-html-and-css/form-layout-tips?u=76281980&t=445)** Making a forum more pleasant to scan and use because fields have been collected into logical groups will improve the end user experience.
>
> **[7:34](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/building-great-forms-with-html-and-css/form-layout-tips?u=76281980&t=454)** If you think carefully before building out your forms, you will meet and exceed the needs of your end users.
>
> **[7:40](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/building-great-forms-with-html-and-css/form-layout-tips?u=76281980&t=460)** Consider these guidelines, knowing that in certain situations you may need to break or bend the rules.
>
> **[7:46](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/building-great-forms-with-html-and-css/form-layout-tips?u=76281980&t=466)** Always think from the user perspective and you'll get a home run every time

#### Styling labels and inputs
> [LinkedIn Learning](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/building-great-forms-with-html-and-css/styling-labels-and-inputs?u=76281980)

>
> **[0:00](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/building-great-forms-with-html-and-css/styling-labels-and-inputs?u=76281980&t=0)** - [Instructor] You don't need labels for your form to work, but it can be considered an accessibility crime not to use them.
>
> **[0:07](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/building-great-forms-with-html-and-css/styling-labels-and-inputs?u=76281980&t=7)** Labels are what signify what the input box is for and it associates them together.
>
> **[0:12](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/building-great-forms-with-html-and-css/styling-labels-and-inputs?u=76281980&t=12)** The use of the label tag is not only semantically correct, but it gives you the opportunity to style them uniquely with CSS.
>
> **[0:20](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/building-great-forms-with-html-and-css/styling-labels-and-inputs?u=76281980&t=20)** Labels are not help texts.
>
> **[0:23](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/building-great-forms-with-html-and-css/styling-labels-and-inputs?u=76281980&t=23)** Use succinct, short, descriptive labels, just a word or two so that users can quickly scan your form.

>
> **[0:18](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/building-great-forms-with-html-and-css/focus-styles?u=76281980&t=18)** The focus state refers to the properties of the form field when the field has been clicked on and is actively being edited.
>
> **[0:25](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/building-great-forms-with-html-and-css/focus-styles?u=76281980&t=25)** The focus indicator is often signified by a focused ring.
>
> **[0:30](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/building-great-forms-with-html-and-css/focus-styles?u=76281980&t=30)** This identifies the currently focused element on your page.
>
> **[0:33](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/building-great-forms-with-html-and-css/focus-styles?u=76281980&t=33)** For users who are unable to use a mouse, this indicator is extremely important because it acts as a stand in for their mouse pointer.
>
> **[0:41](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/building-great-forms-with-html-and-css/focus-styles?u=76281980&t=41)** You can either click in fields to force them to have focus or if you use your tab key and not use the mouse, you will switch fields, and the one that you are clicked in or has focus is very important for a non-sighted user.
>
> **[0:57](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/building-great-forms-with-html-and-css/focus-styles?u=76281980&t=57)** Sometimes the browser's default focus indicator clashes with your design.
>
> **[1:02](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/building-great-forms-with-html-and-css/focus-styles?u=76281980&t=62)** You can use CSS to restyle it.
>
> **[1:05](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/building-great-forms-with-html-and-css/focus-styles?u=76281980&t=65)** Just remember to keep your keyboard users in mind.
>
> **[1:08](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/building-great-forms-with-html-and-css/focus-styles?u=76281980&t=68)** You never want to eliminate the focus indicator because it is so important for a non-visual user.

#### Using pseudo-classes to be more specific
> [LinkedIn Learning](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/building-great-forms-with-html-and-css/using-pseudo-classes-to-be-more-specific?u=76281980)

> [!transcript]- Transcript
>
> **[0:00](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/building-great-forms-with-html-and-css/using-pseudo-classes-to-be-more-specific?u=76281980&t=0)** - [Instructor] Pseudo-class is basically a phantom state, or specific characteristic of an element that can be targeted with CSS.
>
> **[0:08](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/building-great-forms-with-html-and-css/using-pseudo-classes-to-be-more-specific?u=76281980&t=8)** A pseudo-class has information about the element that's in the document tree, but not available through any specified attribute.
>
> **[0:15](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/building-great-forms-with-html-and-css/using-pseudo-classes-to-be-more-specific?u=76281980&t=15)** For example, you may be familiar with colon hover, which allows you to style the hover state of links and buttons.
>
> **[0:22](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/building-great-forms-with-html-and-css/using-pseudo-classes-to-be-more-specific?u=76281980&t=22)** Or the colon first child pseudo-class, which applies to elements which are the first child of their parents.
>
> **[0:29](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/building-great-forms-with-html-and-css/using-pseudo-classes-to-be-more-specific?u=76281980&t=29)** You can get that information from the DOM tree, but there's no specific attribute for either of those.
>
> **[0:36](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/building-great-forms-with-html-and-css/using-pseudo-classes-to-be-more-specific?u=76281980&t=36)** Let's take a look at some pseudo-classes that are specific to form fields, and form field inputs.
>
> **[0:42](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/building-great-forms-with-html-and-css/using-pseudo-classes-to-be-more-specific?u=76281980&t=42)** These pseudo-classes can be used to style fields based on the validity of user input, or whether the field is required, or it's currently enabled.
>
> **[0:51](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/building-great-forms-with-html-and-css/using-pseudo-classes-to-be-more-specific?u=76281980&t=51)** We're going to look at several of these in an actual website.

> **[1:06](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/building-great-forms-with-html-and-css/using-pseudo-classes-to-be-more-specific?u=76281980&t=66)** They're all set up very similarly, in that I have a field set, I have a legend that labels the area, I'll have a label that is attached to the input field, and so on and so forth.

> **[1:26](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/building-great-forms-with-html-and-css/using-pseudo-classes-to-be-more-specific?u=76281980&t=86)** The enabled button, is a button that is enabled, it's ready to use.
>
> **[1:30](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/building-great-forms-with-html-and-css/using-pseudo-classes-to-be-more-specific?u=76281980&t=90)** A disabled button, however, is not clickable, and it's disabled.
>
> **[1:35](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/building-great-forms-with-html-and-css/using-pseudo-classes-to-be-more-specific?u=76281980&t=95)** As you can see, I've styled this to be a little more dim.

>
> **[1:49](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/building-great-forms-with-html-and-css/using-pseudo-classes-to-be-more-specific?u=76281980&t=109)** So I'm reducing the opacity of this element, by using the pseudo-class state.
>
> **[1:55](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/building-great-forms-with-html-and-css/using-pseudo-classes-to-be-more-specific?u=76281980&t=115)** The next pseudo-class state that we want to look at is going to be required and optional.

> **[0:23](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/building-great-forms-with-html-and-css/styling-select-part-2?u=76281980&t=23)** So in order for us to style this, we're going to have to add some JavaScript first because we are going to have JavaScript dynamically create an unordered list which is much more flexible in regards to CSS styling, so that we can style this exactly how we want it to look.
>
> **[0:41](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/building-great-forms-with-html-and-css/styling-select-part-2?u=76281980&t=41)** I'll jump in to my code snippets and I'm going to scroll down to the bottom section of the page where I have this custom JavaScript code.

> **[0:59](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/building-great-forms-with-html-and-css/styling-select-part-2?u=76281980&t=59)** This particular script that I'm using is taking advantage of jQuery.

>
> **[1:37](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/building-great-forms-with-html-and-css/styling-select-part-2?u=76281980&t=97)** So this is a great solution because if the user for whatever reason doesn't have JavaScript on, this degrades really nicely.

> **[1:44](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/building-great-forms-with-html-and-css/styling-select-part-2?u=76281980&t=104)** They'll just see a select box that looks like this, they're not going to not have the option to use the select box.

#### Float labels
> [LinkedIn Learning](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/building-great-forms-with-html-and-css/float-labels?u=76281980)

> **[0:00](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/building-great-forms-with-html-and-css/float-labels?u=76281980&t=0)** - [Instructor] The float label pattern is where an input appears as if it has placeholder text but when you click into that input, the text moves out of the way and allows you to type inside of that field.
>
> **[0:12](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/building-great-forms-with-html-and-css/float-labels?u=76281980&t=12)** The floated label technique has been around for quite a while and the general idea is this.
>
> **[0:18](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/building-great-forms-with-html-and-css/float-labels?u=76281980&t=18)** We have a text input with the label acting as a placeholder attribute.
>
> **[0:22](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/building-great-forms-with-html-and-css/float-labels?u=76281980&t=22)** When a user types into that input, the label moves from inside the input to outside of it.
>

> **[4:59](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/building-great-forms-with-html-and-css/float-labels?u=76281980&t=299)** These values are going to move the label down from the input field in the X, Y coordinates and then when we scale the text up a little bit so it's larger when it's inside of the input fields.
>
> **[5:12](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/building-great-forms-with-html-and-css/float-labels?u=76281980&t=312)** So let's just save this so I can show you what it looks like.
>
> **[5:15](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/building-great-forms-with-html-and-css/float-labels?u=76281980&t=315)** If we refresh the page, you can see how now the labels exist inside of the inputs.
>
> **[5:20](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/building-great-forms-with-html-and-css/float-labels?u=76281980&t=320)** When I click inside of the inputs, my blinking insertion point is just waiting for me to type something and obviously, if I do type something, we're going to get rid of or obscure the label, the label now moves out.
>
> **[5:37](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/building-great-forms-with-html-and-css/float-labels?u=76281980&t=337)** So, we just need one more rule so that the label actually moves out when we click or when we focus in the field.
>
> **[5:45](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/building-great-forms-with-html-and-css/float-labels?u=76281980&t=345)** Let's go back to our snippet file and we'll copy this last bit of code.
>
> **[5:52](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/building-great-forms-with-html-and-css/float-labels?u=76281980&t=352)** I'm going to go back into my CSS and we'll paste this in and what we're doing here is we're using a rule that is using several pseudo classes together.
>
> **[6:02](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/building-great-forms-with-html-and-css/float-labels?u=76281980&t=362)** It's using the not and the placeholders shown in this particular situation and then targeting the label itself.
>
> **[6:10](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/building-great-forms-with-html-and-css/float-labels?u=76281980&t=370)** So when the element is focused, we remove the label transform.
>
> **[6:15](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/building-great-forms-with-html-and-css/float-labels?u=76281980&t=375)** We also do this when the placeholder is not shown.
>
> **[6:19](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/building-great-forms-with-html-and-css/float-labels?u=76281980&t=379)** So when there's something in the input field then we set the cursor to the pointer.
>
> **[6:24](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/building-great-forms-with-html-and-css/float-labels?u=76281980&t=384)** So let's just save this and if we refresh now and we click in any of the fields, you can see how our label is going to move outside of the field and shrink and sit above the input field.
>
> **[6:37](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/building-great-forms-with-html-and-css/float-labels?u=76281980&t=397)** I really like this float label technique I think it's kind of fun and it's very useful because it saves space and it gives the user a little subtle movement when they interact with the fields.
>
> **[6:49](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/building-great-forms-with-html-and-css/float-labels?u=76281980&t=409)** It may not be a solution for every project but I've had great success incorporating it into some of my websites that I've been building lately.

### 4. Login and Registration Form

>
> **[0:07](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/building-great-forms-with-html-and-css/the-registration-process?u=76281980&t=7)** While they could be considered the first barrier to an online sale, they're essential for any business that wants to maintain a relationship with a customer.

>
> **[0:51](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/building-great-forms-with-html-and-css/the-registration-process?u=76281980&t=51)** These are things that you can do to get it right.
>
> **[0:54](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/building-great-forms-with-html-and-css/the-registration-process?u=76281980&t=54)** Add value for registration.
>
> **[0:57](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/building-great-forms-with-html-and-css/the-registration-process?u=76281980&t=57)** Why should your users register?
>
> **[0:59](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/building-great-forms-with-html-and-css/the-registration-process?u=76281980&t=59)** As a service or product provider, you must be able to convince your users why they should spend a few minutes of their time doing so.
>
> **[1:06](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/building-great-forms-with-html-and-css/the-registration-process?u=76281980&t=66)** To do this, show the user the benefits of becoming a registered user.
>
> **[1:11](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/building-great-forms-with-html-and-css/the-registration-process?u=76281980&t=71)** It's proven that doing so will make your users more willing to give their personal details in exchange for what you have to offer.
>
> **[1:19](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/building-great-forms-with-html-and-css/the-registration-process?u=76281980&t=79)** Eliminate unnecessary fields.
>
> **[1:21](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/building-great-forms-with-html-and-css/the-registration-process?u=76281980&t=81)** When registering a new user, ask the minimum you need to get started.
>
> **[1:26](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/building-great-forms-with-html-and-css/the-registration-process?u=76281980&t=86)** You can always ask further information down the line.
>
> **[1:29](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/building-great-forms-with-html-and-css/the-registration-process?u=76281980&t=89)** If your goal is quality and not quantity, you're better off focusing on what your qualitative leads need.
>
> **[1:36](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/building-great-forms-with-html-and-css/the-registration-process?u=76281980&t=96)** One of the things that any registration form can do better is to remove the double entry password and email fields.
>
> **[1:43](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/building-great-forms-with-html-and-css/the-registration-process?u=76281980&t=103)** There are other solutions for capturing typos and wrong inputs that are widely available, and definitely will take the burden off of your end user.
>
> **[1:52](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/building-great-forms-with-html-and-css/the-registration-process?u=76281980&t=112)** Know where you put your labels.
>
> **[1:53](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/building-great-forms-with-html-and-css/the-registration-process?u=76281980&t=113)** Labels above the input fields take less time to fill than left aligned form inputs, because there are fewer visual fixations.
>
> **[2:02](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/building-great-forms-with-html-and-css/the-registration-process?u=76281980&t=122)** Studies also argue that inline top aligned labels are the best way to present labels because they offer minimal visual fixations and make the form lean and compact.
>
> **[2:13](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/building-great-forms-with-html-and-css/the-registration-process?u=76281980&t=133)** In my opinion, the best technique for desktop and mobile is to put labels above each input.
>
> **[2:18](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/building-great-forms-with-html-and-css/the-registration-process?u=76281980&t=138)** This means fewer visual fixations.
>
> **[2:21](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/building-great-forms-with-html-and-css/the-registration-process?u=76281980&t=141)** No visual ambiguity and a cleaner and clearer experience for the end user.
>
> **[2:26](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/building-great-forms-with-html-and-css/the-registration-process?u=76281980&t=146)** Provide guidance.
>
> **[2:28](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/building-great-forms-with-html-and-css/the-registration-process?u=76281980&t=148)** A successful registration form will guide the user through it.
>
> **[2:32](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/building-great-forms-with-html-and-css/the-registration-process?u=76281980&t=152)** You should explain how you want users to input your data, and why you need it.
>
> **[2:37](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/building-great-forms-with-html-and-css/the-registration-process?u=76281980&t=157)** Do you need their birthday?
>
> **[2:38](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/building-great-forms-with-html-and-css/the-registration-process?u=76281980&t=158)** Then tell them why this is.
>
> **[2:40](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/building-great-forms-with-html-and-css/the-registration-process?u=76281980&t=160)** That's going to help to build trust and the user will ultimately thank you for it.
>
> **[2:44](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/building-great-forms-with-html-and-css/the-registration-process?u=76281980&t=164)** Required inputs are bad, use optional ones instead.
>
> **[2:48](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/building-great-forms-with-html-and-css/the-registration-process?u=76281980&t=168)** Required inputs could affect your signup quality conversions.
>
> **[2:52](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/building-great-forms-with-html-and-css/the-registration-process?u=76281980&t=172)** A research study shows that people are willing to provide more information than is needed.
>
> **[2:57](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/building-great-forms-with-html-and-css/the-registration-process?u=76281980&t=177)** That means if you say fields are required, then users will only fill in those fields.
>
> **[3:02](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/building-great-forms-with-html-and-css/the-registration-process?u=76281980&t=182)** Instead of indicating required fields, mark the optional fields and users won't feel the need to take shortcuts to fill out the form.
>
> **[3:10](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/building-great-forms-with-html-and-css/the-registration-process?u=76281980&t=190)** Use the right input field and type attribute.
>
> **[3:13](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/building-great-forms-with-html-and-css/the-registration-process?u=76281980&t=193)** Take advantage of HTML5 and give the proper type attribute to inputs.
>
> **[3:18](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/building-great-forms-with-html-and-css/the-registration-process?u=76281980&t=198)** For example, use the input type of email when you're asking for a user's email.
>
> **[3:24](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/building-great-forms-with-html-and-css/the-registration-process?u=76281980&t=204)** On mobile, the keyboard defaults to a friendly email input, when the user is about to fill in this type of field.
>
> **[3:31](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/building-great-forms-with-html-and-css/the-registration-process?u=76281980&t=211)** It's a little bit beyond the scope of this course, but these are also things that you should consider.
>
> **[3:36](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/building-great-forms-with-html-and-css/the-registration-process?u=76281980&t=216)** Use social media for login.
>
> **[3:38](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/building-great-forms-with-html-and-css/the-registration-process?u=76281980&t=218)** Did you know that the average person has between seven and 25 accounts that they log into every day?
>
> **[3:44](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/building-great-forms-with-html-and-css/the-registration-process?u=76281980&t=224)** And around 82% of people have forgotten passwords used on a website.
>
> **[3:49](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/building-great-forms-with-html-and-css/the-registration-process?u=76281980&t=229)** Not surprisingly, this has led to the expression password fatigue.
>
> **[3:53](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/building-great-forms-with-html-and-css/the-registration-process?u=76281980&t=233)** On the other hand, imagine a user arriving on your website and being able to log in with one click.
>
> **[3:59](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/building-great-forms-with-html-and-css/the-registration-process?u=76281980&t=239)** Following which you can harvest a ton of data from that user.
>
> **[4:03](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/building-great-forms-with-html-and-css/the-registration-process?u=76281980&t=243)** With social media login you can decrease fake email registrations, and personalized online experiences, by using the retrieved data.
>
> **[4:12](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/building-great-forms-with-html-and-css/the-registration-process?u=76281980&t=252)** Also avoid using Captcha.
>
> **[4:14](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/building-great-forms-with-html-and-css/the-registration-process?u=76281980&t=254)** Captcha is probably one of the most major causes of form drop off.
>
> **[4:18](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/building-great-forms-with-html-and-css/the-registration-process?u=76281980&t=258)** As such, it does more harm than good.
>
> **[4:21](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/building-great-forms-with-html-and-css/the-registration-process?u=76281980&t=261)** There are alternatives out there which don't have any negative impact on your form.
>
> **[4:26](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/building-great-forms-with-html-and-css/the-registration-process?u=76281980&t=266)** Use an alternative, like the Google solution of reCaptcha or use the honeypot technique, which consists of having an input field that the users leave blank.
>
> **[4:36](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/building-great-forms-with-html-and-css/the-registration-process?u=76281980&t=276)** A robot will fill it in.
>
> **[4:38](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/building-great-forms-with-html-and-css/the-registration-process?u=76281980&t=278)** And that's how you can prevent spam.
>
> **[4:41](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/building-great-forms-with-html-and-css/the-registration-process?u=76281980&t=281)** So now you can see how from the surface, a login or registration form seems fairly simple, but in actuality, it has quite a bit of depth and complexity.
>
> **[4:51](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/building-great-forms-with-html-and-css/the-registration-process?u=76281980&t=291)** There are a lot of things you can do to simplify the process and help to meet your end users needs.

#### Login form flow chart and landing page

> [!transcript]- Transcript
>
> **[0:00](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/building-great-forms-with-html-and-css/login-form-flow-chart-and-landing-page?u=76281980&t=0)** - [Instructor] On the surface, Log ins forms seem simple.
>
> **[0:04](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/building-great-forms-with-html-and-css/login-form-flow-chart-and-landing-page?u=76281980&t=4)** They're just two to four fields.
>
> **[0:06](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/building-great-forms-with-html-and-css/login-form-flow-chart-and-landing-page?u=76281980&t=6)** That's it, but before you actually build out a form, it's important to think through the process.
>
> **[0:12](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/building-great-forms-with-html-and-css/login-form-flow-chart-and-landing-page?u=76281980&t=12)** You want to consider all of the possible scenarios and make sure that you have built-out functionality to handle errors and successes.
>
> **[0:20](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/building-great-forms-with-html-and-css/login-form-flow-chart-and-landing-page?u=76281980&t=20)** I like to create a flow diagram to ensure that I have covered any potential screens or situations that may impact my users.
>
> **[0:28](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/building-great-forms-with-html-and-css/login-form-flow-chart-and-landing-page?u=76281980&t=28)** For a log in form we're going to be building, we have a flow diagram that looks something like this.
>
> **[0:34](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/building-great-forms-with-html-and-css/login-form-flow-chart-and-landing-page?u=76281980&t=34)** The user starts, and they come to a screen, and they need to determine are they already a member.
>
> **[0:39](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/building-great-forms-with-html-and-css/login-form-flow-chart-and-landing-page?u=76281980&t=39)** If they are, they can enter their user name and password, and if it's valid, they can successfully log in.
>
> **[0:45](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/building-great-forms-with-html-and-css/login-form-flow-chart-and-landing-page?u=76281980&t=45)** If it's not valid, then did they forget their password?
>
> **[0:50](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/building-great-forms-with-html-and-css/login-form-flow-chart-and-landing-page?u=76281980&t=50)** If yes, they need to reset it, and then they can enter their user name and password again, or if their credentials were not valid and the log in failed, then did they forget the password and do they need to reset it?
>
> **[1:03](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/building-great-forms-with-html-and-css/login-form-flow-chart-and-landing-page?u=76281980&t=63)** Or do they just need to login again.
>
> **[1:06](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/building-great-forms-with-html-and-css/login-form-flow-chart-and-landing-page?u=76281980&t=66)** If they aren't a member, then they need to register, and if they register, they have to fill in the registration form.
>
> **[1:13](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/building-great-forms-with-html-and-css/login-form-flow-chart-and-landing-page?u=76281980&t=73)** It needs to authenticate, and then once that's done, they can begin by logging in, and it'll check the credentials.
>
> **[1:21](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/building-great-forms-with-html-and-css/login-form-flow-chart-and-landing-page?u=76281980&t=81)** Obviously this is a very simple example, but it does help to be able to diagram out these basic areas.
>
> **[1:28](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/building-great-forms-with-html-and-css/login-form-flow-chart-and-landing-page?u=76281980&t=88)** Once you have these basic areas specified, then you can begin by building out the form pages.
>
> **[1:34](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/building-great-forms-with-html-and-css/login-form-flow-chart-and-landing-page?u=76281980&t=94)** You need to start with a basic page that gives the user the option to log in if they're already a member or to register if they're new.

> **[1:42](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/building-great-forms-with-html-and-css/login-form-flow-chart-and-landing-page?u=76281980&t=102)** But usually before you even get to that page, you probably need a landing page, some sort of call to action.

>
> **[1:54](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/building-great-forms-with-html-and-css/login-form-flow-chart-and-landing-page?u=76281980&t=114)** Note that it's important to be clear with your users what action they will be taking.
>

>
> **[2:09](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/building-great-forms-with-html-and-css/login-form-flow-chart-and-landing-page?u=76281980&t=129)** So if they're already a member, they can immediately go and sign in.
>

>
> **[2:24](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/building-great-forms-with-html-and-css/login-form-flow-chart-and-landing-page?u=76281980&t=144)** These pages don't have any form elements.
>
> **[2:27](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/building-great-forms-with-html-and-css/login-form-flow-chart-and-landing-page?u=76281980&t=147)** They just provide a clean and easy way for our user to either log in or register.

> **[2:32](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/building-great-forms-with-html-and-css/login-form-flow-chart-and-landing-page?u=76281980&t=152)** We will use many of these styles and similar html formatting on our form-based pages.
>
> **[2:38](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/building-great-forms-with-html-and-css/login-form-flow-chart-and-landing-page?u=76281980&t=158)** We'll be building these out in the next upcoming videos.

#### Building the login page
> [LinkedIn Learning](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/building-great-forms-with-html-and-css/building-the-login-page?u=76281980)

> [!transcript]- Transcript
>

#### Using tooltips for more information
> [LinkedIn Learning](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/building-great-forms-with-html-and-css/using-tooltips-for-more-information?u=76281980)

> [!transcript]- Transcript
>
> **[0:00](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/building-great-forms-with-html-and-css/using-tooltips-for-more-information?u=76281980&t=0)** - [Instructor] A tooltip is a brief informative message that appears when a user interacts with an element in a graphical user interface.
>
> **[0:08](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/building-great-forms-with-html-and-css/using-tooltips-for-more-information?u=76281980&t=8)** Tooltips are generally on demand, meaning that when the user hover, clicks, taps or focuses on the trigger, then the message is displayed.
>
> **[0:17](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/building-great-forms-with-html-and-css/using-tooltips-for-more-information?u=76281980&t=17)** Although tooltips are mainly limited to desktop computers and laptops because they're generally initiated by hover, they do have a sister element that is common on touch screen devices.
>
> **[0:28](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/building-great-forms-with-html-and-css/using-tooltips-for-more-information?u=76281980&t=28)** Those are called popup tips.
>
> **[0:30](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/building-great-forms-with-html-and-css/using-tooltips-for-more-information?u=76281980&t=30)** Both tooltips and popup tips have the same goal, to provide helpful additional content.
>
> **[0:36](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/building-great-forms-with-html-and-css/using-tooltips-for-more-information?u=76281980&t=36)** To keep things simple here, I'm going to refer to these as help tips.
>
> **[0:41](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/building-great-forms-with-html-and-css/using-tooltips-for-more-information?u=76281980&t=41)** In our Sea Garden website, we'll be adding help tips to the Password field so that we can give a little further explanation on how the user should enter the password, and on our City field, just telling our user why we might want to collect this information.
>
> **[0:57](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/building-great-forms-with-html-and-css/using-tooltips-for-more-information?u=76281980&t=57)** Tooltips are very handy because you can tuck away further information and not have it cluttering up your interface but still have it readily available for your users.
>
> **[1:08](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/building-great-forms-with-html-and-css/using-tooltips-for-more-information?u=76281980&t=68)** This works best with information that might not be necessary for everyone but can be beneficial to some users if they need more direction.

> **[12:20](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/building-great-forms-with-html-and-css/using-tooltips-for-more-information?u=76281980&t=740)** Furnishing the user with some sort of explanation of what a particular option means or why we are asking for it can help make the form less incomprehensible.
>
> **[12:31](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/building-great-forms-with-html-and-css/using-tooltips-for-more-information?u=76281980&t=751)** Tooltips are an on demand option which can be present, but tucked away until the user interacts with them or needs them.
>
> **[12:38](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/building-great-forms-with-html-and-css/using-tooltips-for-more-information?u=76281980&t=758)** This is really a great option for usability since it provides the user with more information if they decide they need it, and eliminates unnecessary clutter and elements on your page.

### 5. A Booking Form: Flight, Hotel, Movie, and More

#### What is a booking form?
> [LinkedIn Learning](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/building-great-forms-with-html-and-css/what-is-a-booking-form?u=76281980)

> [!transcript]- Transcript
>
> **[0:00](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/building-great-forms-with-html-and-css/what-is-a-booking-form?u=76281980&t=0)** - [Instructor] In this section of the course, we will be designing a flight booking service.
>
> **[0:04](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/building-great-forms-with-html-and-css/what-is-a-booking-form?u=76281980&t=4)** At first glance, this may seem a bit niche especially when compared to a registration form or a checkout form.
>
> **[0:11](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/building-great-forms-with-html-and-css/what-is-a-booking-form?u=76281980&t=11)** However, we're going to explore several complex problems that in the end will result in a reusable pattern.
>
> **[0:18](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/building-great-forms-with-html-and-css/what-is-a-booking-form?u=76281980&t=18)** Patterns that are very much transferable to other problems, such as booking a movie ticket, a tour, a popular event, making an online appointment, or even booking a hotel room or booking a flight form is going to consist of many discreet steps.
>
> **[0:33](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/building-great-forms-with-html-and-css/what-is-a-booking-form?u=76281980&t=33)** The first few steps simply collect the user's preference.
>
> **[0:37](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/building-great-forms-with-html-and-css/what-is-a-booking-form?u=76281980&t=37)** Where to fly, when to fly and how many people are flying.
>
> **[0:41](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/building-great-forms-with-html-and-css/what-is-a-booking-form?u=76281980&t=41)** Once we have that information, we can give the users a choice of available flights followed by choosing where to sit and finally users will need to enter their details and make a payment.
>
> **[0:51](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/building-great-forms-with-html-and-css/what-is-a-booking-form?u=76281980&t=51)** We'll be covering payment forms later on in the course.

#### Creating custom radio buttons
> [LinkedIn Learning](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/building-great-forms-with-html-and-css/creating-custom-radio-buttons?u=76281980)

> [!transcript]- Transcript
>
> **[0:00](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/building-great-forms-with-html-and-css/creating-custom-radio-buttons?u=76281980&t=0)** - [Instructor] A radio button is a form element that allows the user to select one option from a range of options.
>
> **[0:06](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/building-great-forms-with-html-and-css/creating-custom-radio-buttons?u=76281980&t=6)** Radio buttons are created with HTML input tag, and then we set the type to radio.
>
> **[0:12](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/building-great-forms-with-html-and-css/creating-custom-radio-buttons?u=76281980&t=12)** It's important to understand the difference between radio buttons and check boxes.
>
> **[0:16](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/building-great-forms-with-html-and-css/creating-custom-radio-buttons?u=76281980&t=16)** Check boxes differ from radio buttons in that check boxes allow a user to select any number of options, whereas radio buttons only allow the user to select one option.
>
> **[0:26](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/building-great-forms-with-html-and-css/creating-custom-radio-buttons?u=76281980&t=26)** So radio buttons are better suited when we want the user to select one and only one.
>
> **[0:32](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/building-great-forms-with-html-and-css/creating-custom-radio-buttons?u=76281980&t=32)** So in our form here, we only want them to select one option from this group.
>
> **[0:38](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/building-great-forms-with-html-and-css/creating-custom-radio-buttons?u=76281980&t=38)** Now, these are how the radio buttons currently work.

> **[6:59](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/building-great-forms-with-html-and-css/creating-custom-radio-buttons?u=76281980&t=419)** Now, we do have a little bit of an alignment problem with our radio buttons.
>
> **[7:04](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/building-great-forms-with-html-and-css/creating-custom-radio-buttons?u=76281980&t=424)** They are working in the way that we want, but the alignment is not correct, and what we actually want to do is when the page is about this wide, we want these to appear on one line rather than being stacked.
>
> **[7:16](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/building-great-forms-with-html-and-css/creating-custom-radio-buttons?u=76281980&t=436)** We only want them to be stacked when the page is smaller.
>
> **[7:20](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/building-great-forms-with-html-and-css/creating-custom-radio-buttons?u=76281980&t=440)** So let me just open my developer tools.
>
> **[7:22](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/building-great-forms-with-html-and-css/creating-custom-radio-buttons?u=76281980&t=442)** I'm going to right-click and choose Inspect, and if the page is a little bit more narrow, you can see that these radio buttons stack and they look beautiful.
>
> **[7:33](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/building-great-forms-with-html-and-css/creating-custom-radio-buttons?u=76281980&t=453)** This is exactly the way we'd like them to appear.
>
> **[7:36](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/building-great-forms-with-html-and-css/creating-custom-radio-buttons?u=76281980&t=456)** But when the page gets a little bit wider, then we have this funky alignment.
>
> **[7:40](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/building-great-forms-with-html-and-css/creating-custom-radio-buttons?u=76281980&t=460)** The funky alignment is a result of an existing rule that we have on labels.
>
> **[7:46](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/building-great-forms-with-html-and-css/creating-custom-radio-buttons?u=76281980&t=466)** So if we come down here into the inspector, you can see that I'm selecting on one of the labels, and we have a rule that is in our base file on the media query that is setting the margin left to 15 pixels.
>
> **[8:05](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/building-great-forms-with-html-and-css/creating-custom-radio-buttons?u=76281980&t=485)** So we need that rule for some of our other elements, but we do want to address at the wider screen having these elements sit side by side.
>
> **[8:14](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/building-great-forms-with-html-and-css/creating-custom-radio-buttons?u=76281980&t=494)** I'm going to jump into my code snippet file, and I'm going to grab this code down at the bottom, the media query code, and we'll copy this into our CSS document.
>
> **[8:26](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/building-great-forms-with-html-and-css/creating-custom-radio-buttons?u=76281980&t=506)** And this first rule, we actually don't need this, because this rule is already being applied in our base style.
>
> **[8:33](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/building-great-forms-with-html-and-css/creating-custom-radio-buttons?u=76281980&t=513)** So this is kind of redundant, I'll just get rid of that.
>
> **[8:36](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/building-great-forms-with-html-and-css/creating-custom-radio-buttons?u=76281980&t=516)** And what we will do, though, is we'll use this rule on the labels for the flight book trip type, which is isolating the labels that are within our radio button group, and we're going to display them as inline block, which will have them displaying side by side.
>
> **[8:54](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/building-great-forms-with-html-and-css/creating-custom-radio-buttons?u=76281980&t=534)** So if we go ahead and refresh our page, you can see that at the larger screen size, these will sit side by side, and when the screen is more narrow, they will then stack to a vertical orientation.
>
> **[9:07](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/building-great-forms-with-html-and-css/creating-custom-radio-buttons?u=76281980&t=547)** So our form looks pretty good like this, our radio buttons are now customized.
>
> **[9:11](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/building-great-forms-with-html-and-css/creating-custom-radio-buttons?u=76281980&t=551)** They have this great little animation, and as you can see, by customizing the booking form, we're able to really design radio buttons that are bigger, they're easier to click, and they just work better with our form.
>
> **[9:24](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/building-great-forms-with-html-and-css/creating-custom-radio-buttons?u=76281980&t=564)** They align with the look and the feel of our form much better than what we had before.

#### Dates and date pickers

>
> **[0:10](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/building-great-forms-with-html-and-css/dates-and-date-pickers?u=76281980&t=10)** A date input control is a text control where the user can insert a date in a required format.
>
> **[0:17](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/building-great-forms-with-html-and-css/dates-and-date-pickers?u=76281980&t=17)** It's helpful for the user to provide some sort of guidance about that required format.
>
> **[0:22](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/building-great-forms-with-html-and-css/dates-and-date-pickers?u=76281980&t=22)** A date picker control is used where a data's required as input from a user, for example, a date of birth, a joining date, a range of date for searching required items, et cetera.
>
> **[0:35](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/building-great-forms-with-html-and-css/dates-and-date-pickers?u=76281980&t=35)** The date picker in HTML5 basically works very similarly to how the JavaScript libraries did.

### 6. Payment Forms

>
> **[1:07](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/building-great-forms-with-html-and-css/organizing-a-payment-form-2?u=76281980&t=67)** To give your visitors the best possible experience, your designs should be simple.
>
> **[1:12](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/building-great-forms-with-html-and-css/organizing-a-payment-form-2?u=76281980&t=72)** It pays off to follow the less is more philosophy when it comes to payment forms.
>
> **[1:17](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/building-great-forms-with-html-and-css/organizing-a-payment-form-2?u=76281980&t=77)** This has been proven time and time again in studies that visually complex designs is seen as generally unappealing and psychology we feel inclined to interact with a page that's emptier.
>
> **[1:28](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/building-great-forms-with-html-and-css/organizing-a-payment-form-2?u=76281980&t=88)** So the length of your form does matter.
>
> **[1:31](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/building-great-forms-with-html-and-css/organizing-a-payment-form-2?u=76281980&t=91)** Offer multiple payment methods.
>

>

>
> **[1:43](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/building-great-forms-with-html-and-css/organizing-a-payment-form-2?u=76281980&t=103)** It's convenient and much more flexible than limiting customers to one form of payment.
>

>

>
> **[2:00](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/building-great-forms-with-html-and-css/organizing-a-payment-form-2?u=76281980&t=120)** You don't want to prevent someone from paying you.
>

>

>
> **[2:11](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/building-great-forms-with-html-and-css/organizing-a-payment-form-2?u=76281980&t=131)** If a form has 30 or more fields, and they're all in one place, that can be very overwhelming.
>

>
> **[2:28](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/building-great-forms-with-html-and-css/organizing-a-payment-form-2?u=76281980&t=148)** This will ensure that the end user is not overwhelmed with too many steps.

>

>
> **[2:42](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/building-great-forms-with-html-and-css/organizing-a-payment-form-2?u=76281980&t=162)** One thing per page is about splitting up a complicated process into small chunks and placing them on screens of their own.

>

>

>
> **[3:27](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/building-great-forms-with-html-and-css/organizing-a-payment-form-2?u=76281980&t=207)** Communicate clearly on your website, and your customers will be happier.
>
> **[3:30](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/building-great-forms-with-html-and-css/organizing-a-payment-form-2?u=76281980&t=210)** Payment forms are not the place for vague questions or confusing language.

>
> **[3:51](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/building-great-forms-with-html-and-css/organizing-a-payment-form-2?u=76281980&t=231)** Strive to give the user only what they need and give it to them at the appropriate time.

#### Flow and order
> [LinkedIn Learning](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/building-great-forms-with-html-and-css/flow-and-order?u=76281980)

> [!transcript]- Transcript
>
> **[0:00](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/building-great-forms-with-html-and-css/flow-and-order?u=76281980&t=0)** - [Instructor] One other concept that we need to discuss before we get started building our forms is flow and order.
>
> **[0:05](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/building-great-forms-with-html-and-css/flow-and-order?u=76281980&t=5)** This has to do with how the user goes through the form.
>
> **[0:09](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/building-great-forms-with-html-and-css/flow-and-order?u=76281980&t=9)** The first thing you want to think about is asking questions in a sensible order.
>
> **[0:14](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/building-great-forms-with-html-and-css/flow-and-order?u=76281980&t=14)** That sounds reasonable, right?
>
> **[0:16](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/building-great-forms-with-html-and-css/flow-and-order?u=76281980&t=16)** Why is is that so many forms aren't doing that correctly?
>
> **[0:20](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/building-great-forms-with-html-and-css/flow-and-order?u=76281980&t=20)** Asking for information at the wrong time can alienate a user.
>
> **[0:24](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/building-great-forms-with-html-and-css/flow-and-order?u=76281980&t=24)** The same question put at the right moment can be entirely acceptable.
>
> **[0:28](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/building-great-forms-with-html-and-css/flow-and-order?u=76281980&t=28)** Ask for the right information at the right time.
>
> **[0:31](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/building-great-forms-with-html-and-css/flow-and-order?u=76281980&t=31)** For example, payment happens towards the end.
>
> **[0:34](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/building-great-forms-with-html-and-css/flow-and-order?u=76281980&t=34)** Users will be given a chance to check their order before submitting it.
>
> **[0:38](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/building-great-forms-with-html-and-css/flow-and-order?u=76281980&t=38)** And finally, the confirmation page acts as a sales receipt.
>
> **[0:41](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/building-great-forms-with-html-and-css/flow-and-order?u=76281980&t=41)** Here's a complete flow.
>
> **[0:44](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/building-great-forms-with-html-and-css/flow-and-order?u=76281980&t=44)** Ask for an email address, then a mobile phone.
>
> **[0:47](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/building-great-forms-with-html-and-css/flow-and-order?u=76281980&t=47)** Maybe that's optional.
>
> **[0:49](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/building-great-forms-with-html-and-css/flow-and-order?u=76281980&t=49)** You'll need their delivery address.
>
> **[0:51](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/building-great-forms-with-html-and-css/flow-and-order?u=76281980&t=51)** They'll need to specify delivery options and maybe add some delivery notes.
>
> **[0:55](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/building-great-forms-with-html-and-css/flow-and-order?u=76281980&t=55)** You'll need to have your user check their answers and then you'll go ahead and offer payment and confirmation.
>
> **[1:02](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/building-great-forms-with-html-and-css/flow-and-order?u=76281980&t=62)** Explain why you're asking for certain data.
>
> **[1:05](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/building-great-forms-with-html-and-css/flow-and-order?u=76281980&t=65)** Use hints; this is very helpful.
>
> **[1:08](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/building-great-forms-with-html-and-css/flow-and-order?u=76281980&t=68)** Transparency builds trust, it reduces effort, and it promotes the features, all at the same time.
>
> **[1:15](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/building-great-forms-with-html-and-css/flow-and-order?u=76281980&t=75)** For instance, we can send users a receipt, which is particularly important if checking out anonymously.
>
> **[1:22](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/building-great-forms-with-html-and-css/flow-and-order?u=76281980&t=82)** So that's why we'll need to have their email.
>
> **[1:25](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/building-great-forms-with-html-and-css/flow-and-order?u=76281980&t=85)** Additionally, the email might provide details about how to return the item or cancel or change the order.
>
> **[1:32](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/building-great-forms-with-html-and-css/flow-and-order?u=76281980&t=92)** A mobile number is used to text notifications about the delivery, so that might be important.
>
> **[1:38](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/building-great-forms-with-html-and-css/flow-and-order?u=76281980&t=98)** While real-time notifications add value, we shouldn't assume everyone wants to receive them, nor that everyone has a mobile phone.
>
> **[1:46](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/building-great-forms-with-html-and-css/flow-and-order?u=76281980&t=106)** So we let users choose to skip this field by marking the field as optional.
>
> **[1:51](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/building-great-forms-with-html-and-css/flow-and-order?u=76281980&t=111)** Really think through your process.
>
> **[1:54](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/building-great-forms-with-html-and-css/flow-and-order?u=76281980&t=114)** Think not only of the steps, but the necessary information that you actually need.
>
> **[1:59](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/building-great-forms-with-html-and-css/flow-and-order?u=76281980&t=119)** Don't burden the user with unnecessary fields.
>
> **[2:02](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/building-great-forms-with-html-and-css/flow-and-order?u=76281980&t=122)** Make it easy, clear, and concise.
>
> **[2:05](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/building-great-forms-with-html-and-css/flow-and-order?u=76281980&t=125)** Your goal is for a transparent online shopping experience.

>
> **[5:28](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/building-great-forms-with-html-and-css/input-mask?u=76281980&t=328)** We're also using the match function and this is another inbuilt function in JavaScript.

> **[5:33](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/building-great-forms-with-html-and-css/input-mask?u=76281980&t=333)** It's used to search a string for a match against any regular expression and if the match is found then it will return the match as an array.
>
> **[5:43](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/building-great-forms-with-html-and-css/input-mask?u=76281980&t=343)** The following part of our code is where we match.
>
> **[5:46](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/building-great-forms-with-html-and-css/input-mask?u=76281980&t=346)** So the backslash D matches any digit character in patterns of three, three and four.
>
> **[5:57](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/building-great-forms-with-html-and-css/input-mask?u=76281980&t=357)** Then we have e.target.value and this line is where we change the value to our preferred formatting.
>
> **[6:06](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/building-great-forms-with-html-and-css/input-mask?u=76281980&t=366)** So here we're using a ternary operator which is basically a shorthand method of writing an if L statement.
>
> **[6:13](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/building-great-forms-with-html-and-css/input-mask?u=76281980&t=373)** So this is testing to see if three numeric characters have been entered and if so we add the parenthesis and we also are including a space here.
>
> **[6:25](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/building-great-forms-with-html-and-css/input-mask?u=76281980&t=385)** After the next three characters are entered we're going to go ahead and print a dash and then we're going to print the remaining four characters.
>
> **[6:35](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/building-great-forms-with-html-and-css/input-mask?u=76281980&t=395)** So if we save our page and we go back out to the browser and refresh if I start to enter any numbers into the phone number value as soon as I hit the fourth number you can see that my formatting is kicking in and it will continue to format the numbers in the way that we specified.
>
> **[6:52](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/building-great-forms-with-html-and-css/input-mask?u=76281980&t=412)** If I try to enter any additional values it won't let me and if I try to enter any characters it won't let me.
>
> **[7:00](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/building-great-forms-with-html-and-css/input-mask?u=76281980&t=420)** So this field will only accept the values that we specified which are numeric values.
>
> **[7:07](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/building-great-forms-with-html-and-css/input-mask?u=76281980&t=427)** This is a really great technique and let me show you what this looks like on a phone.
>
> **[7:13](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/building-great-forms-with-html-and-css/input-mask?u=76281980&t=433)** So here's the page on a phone and if I scroll down to the Phone field and click in the Phone field you'll see that the keyboard that comes up is the numeric keyboard.
>
> **[7:26](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/building-great-forms-with-html-and-css/input-mask?u=76281980&t=446)** So this is really great because it prevents the user from having to switch to a different keypad and this is true with any of our other elements as well.
>
> **[7:36](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/building-great-forms-with-html-and-css/input-mask?u=76281980&t=456)** Like if I click in State for instance we get the regular keypad.

>
> **[7:54](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/building-great-forms-with-html-and-css/input-mask?u=76281980&t=474)** So this is where you can really see the results of utilizing the correct input type when you're creating your input elements.
>
> **[8:03](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/building-great-forms-with-html-and-css/input-mask?u=76281980&t=483)** Now our Phone number field is automatically formatting the telephone number into the expected format.

> **[8:09](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/building-great-forms-with-html-and-css/input-mask?u=76281980&t=489)** This is really helpful to our users and it makes our form that much user friendly.
