> **[4:17](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/building-great-forms-with-html-and-css/review-and-confirmation-pages-2?u=76281980&t=257)** And now I'm on my payment page and I can go about adding the information that I need to enter so that I can get my product as soon as possible.
>
> **[4:27](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/building-great-forms-with-html-and-css/review-and-confirmation-pages-2?u=76281980&t=267)** I'm not filling out every field, but I'm just giving you an idea of some of the fields that we have coded in our form to make sure that you're aware of how they might be working.
>
> **[4:37](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/building-great-forms-with-html-and-css/review-and-confirmation-pages-2?u=76281980&t=277)** Finally, when I'm all done and I complete the order, I'll end up on that thank you page.
>
> **[4:42](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/building-great-forms-with-html-and-css/review-and-confirmation-pages-2?u=76281980&t=282)** As you can see, building a payment form has a lot of nuances.
>
> **[4:46](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/building-great-forms-with-html-and-css/review-and-confirmation-pages-2?u=76281980&t=286)** There's a lot of little things that you need to consider and think about in order that you're making the best experience for your end user.
>
> **[4:53](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/building-great-forms-with-html-and-css/review-and-confirmation-pages-2?u=76281980&t=293)** Just make sure when you're building out longer forms that you break it up into more digestible steps and that you're always thinking with the user in mind.
>
> **[5:02](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/building-great-forms-with-html-and-css/review-and-confirmation-pages-2?u=76281980&t=302)** If you keep these two key ingredients in mind, you should always end up with something that's going to be a success.

### 7. Search Form and Filter Forms

#### Search forms: What are they?
> [LinkedIn Learning](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/building-great-forms-with-html-and-css/search-forms-what-are-they?u=76281980)

> [!transcript]- Transcript
>
> **[0:00](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/building-great-forms-with-html-and-css/search-forms-what-are-they?u=76281980&t=0)** - [Narrator] What is search?
>
> **[0:02](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/building-great-forms-with-html-and-css/search-forms-what-are-they?u=76281980&t=2)** Incorporating a search feature streamlines navigation.
>
> **[0:05](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/building-great-forms-with-html-and-css/search-forms-what-are-they?u=76281980&t=5)** It increases user interaction, lengthens visit time, and provides valuable data about your users' preferences.
>
> **[0:12](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/building-great-forms-with-html-and-css/search-forms-what-are-they?u=76281980&t=12)** It's always best to provide as many ways to find information as possible.
>
> **[0:17](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/building-great-forms-with-html-and-css/search-forms-what-are-they?u=76281980&t=17)** It also cuts down on the number of clicks visitors have to register before finding the data they want.
>
> **[0:23](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/building-great-forms-with-html-and-css/search-forms-what-are-they?u=76281980&t=23)** In almost all cases, adding a search to your site will always be beneficial.
>
> **[0:28](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/building-great-forms-with-html-and-css/search-forms-what-are-they?u=76281980&t=28)** In regards to adding search to your website, you have some options.
>
> **[0:32](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/building-great-forms-with-html-and-css/search-forms-what-are-they?u=76281980&t=32)** The most common search boxes are scripted in coding languages such as PHP or Perl.
>
> **[0:38](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/building-great-forms-with-html-and-css/search-forms-what-are-they?u=76281980&t=38)** The code is housed on the server, and when information is typed in a box and sent, the server-side engine runs the script and produces the results.
>
> **[0:47](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/building-great-forms-with-html-and-css/search-forms-what-are-they?u=76281980&t=47)** Some search scripts will run a crawl on your entire website each time a search is made by a user.
>
> **[0:54](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/building-great-forms-with-html-and-css/search-forms-what-are-they?u=76281980&t=54)** Other scripts will periodically crawl your site and create a search index, and it is this index that is searched whenever a user inputs a search query.
>
> **[1:03](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/building-great-forms-with-html-and-css/search-forms-what-are-they?u=76281980&t=63)** Both methods have advantages and drawbacks, so it's important to consider your needs before deciding.
>
> **[1:09](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/building-great-forms-with-html-and-css/search-forms-what-are-they?u=76281980&t=69)** Another option for website owners is to the utilize a search feature from a third party.
>
> **[1:15](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/building-great-forms-with-html-and-css/search-forms-what-are-they?u=76281980&t=75)** Many internet companies out there will have products where the code can simply be copied and pasted in an appropriate area, and then the search box becomes functional.
>
> **[1:24](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/building-great-forms-with-html-and-css/search-forms-what-are-they?u=76281980&t=84)** Advantages are quick and easy setup, no knowledge of code required, but you may have to sacrifice control over how the search works and in what method the results are displayed.
>
> **[1:35](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/building-great-forms-with-html-and-css/search-forms-what-are-they?u=76281980&t=95)** Finally, you can use a service such as Google's Custom Search to build a basic search feature.
>
> **[1:42](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/building-great-forms-with-html-and-css/search-forms-what-are-they?u=76281980&t=102)** Google offers a free feature that offers limited customization and includes advertisements and also a customizable version that gives you a little bit more control.
>
> **[1:52](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/building-great-forms-with-html-and-css/search-forms-what-are-they?u=76281980&t=112)** You can remove the Google branding and ads, but you'll have to pay a little more money for it each year.
>
> **[1:58](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/building-great-forms-with-html-and-css/search-forms-what-are-they?u=76281980&t=118)** A search function is a helpful tool for both your visitors and your business, and there are many options out there to suit a wide variety of needs.

> **CLI Commands:** find (1), php (1)
> **Code Keywords:** finally, (1), function (1)
> **Analogies:** such as (2)
> **Env Vars:** php (1)
> **Definitions:** is a  (1)
> **Prerequisites:** setup (1)

#### Tips for adding search
> [LinkedIn Learning](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/building-great-forms-with-html-and-css/tips-for-adding-search?u=76281980)

> [!transcript]- Transcript
>
> **[0:00](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/building-great-forms-with-html-and-css/tips-for-adding-search?u=76281980&t=0)** - [Instructor] A search box is a combination of an input field and a submit button.
>
> **[0:04](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/building-great-forms-with-html-and-css/tips-for-adding-search?u=76281980&t=4)** One may think that the search box doesn't need a design.
>
> **[0:08](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/building-great-forms-with-html-and-css/tips-for-adding-search?u=76281980&t=8)** After all, it's just two simple elements.
>
> **[0:11](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/building-great-forms-with-html-and-css/tips-for-adding-search?u=76281980&t=11)** But since the search box is one of the most frequently used design elements on content heavy websites, its usability is critical.
>
> **[0:19](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/building-great-forms-with-html-and-css/tips-for-adding-search?u=76281980&t=19)** When incorporating search, keep these things in mind.
>
> **[0:23](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/building-great-forms-with-html-and-css/tips-for-adding-search?u=76281980&t=23)** Display search field prominently.
>
> **[0:25](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/building-great-forms-with-html-and-css/tips-for-adding-search?u=76281980&t=25)** The most important rule in search box designs is to make it easily noticeable.
>
> **[0:30](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/building-great-forms-with-html-and-css/tips-for-adding-search?u=76281980&t=30)** If search is an important function for your app or a website, it should be displayed prominently, as it can be the fastest route to discovery.
>
> **[0:39](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/building-great-forms-with-html-and-css/tips-for-adding-search?u=76281980&t=39)** Always accompany a search box with a magnifying glass icon.
>
> **[0:43](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/building-great-forms-with-html-and-css/tips-for-adding-search?u=76281980&t=43)** Icons are, by definition, a visual representation of an object, action, or idea.
>
> **[0:49](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/building-great-forms-with-html-and-css/tips-for-adding-search?u=76281980&t=49)** There are a few icons that have mostly universal recognition from users.
>
> **[0:54](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/building-great-forms-with-html-and-css/tips-for-adding-search?u=76281980&t=54)** The magnifying glass icon is one such icon.
>
> **[0:57](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/building-great-forms-with-html-and-css/tips-for-adding-search?u=76281980&t=57)** Users recognize a magnifying glass icon as meaning search, even without a text label.
>
> **[1:03](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/building-great-forms-with-html-and-css/tips-for-adding-search?u=76281980&t=63)** Provide a search button for the search box.
>
> **[1:06](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/building-great-forms-with-html-and-css/tips-for-adding-search?u=76281980&t=66)** Despite the fact that search can be easily triggered by hitting Enter, some users will still search for a more traditional submit button.
>
> **[1:14](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/building-great-forms-with-html-and-css/tips-for-adding-search?u=76281980&t=74)** A button also helps people recognize that there's an additional step to trigger the search action, even if they decide to do this by pressing Enter.
>
> **[1:23](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/building-great-forms-with-html-and-css/tips-for-adding-search?u=76281980&t=83)** Proper field size.
>
> **[1:25](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/building-great-forms-with-html-and-css/tips-for-adding-search?u=76281980&t=85)** Making the input field too short is a common mistake among designers.
>
> **[1:29](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/building-great-forms-with-html-and-css/tips-for-adding-search?u=76281980&t=89)** Of course, users can still type long queries in a short field, but only a portion of the text will be visible at any given time and this equates to bad usability.
>
> **[1:40](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/building-great-forms-with-html-and-css/tips-for-adding-search?u=76281980&t=100)** Since users cannot review or edit their full query, it can cause confusion.
>
> **[1:44](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/building-great-forms-with-html-and-css/tips-for-adding-search?u=76281980&t=104)** In fact, when a search box has a limited number of visible characters, users tend to use short, imprecise queries since longer queries become inconvenient to read.
>
> **[1:54](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/building-great-forms-with-html-and-css/tips-for-adding-search?u=76281980&t=114)** If input fields are sized according to the expected input, they are both easier to read and to interpret for the user.
>
> **[2:02](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/building-great-forms-with-html-and-css/tips-for-adding-search?u=76281980&t=122)** A rule of thumb is to have a 27 character text input.
>
> **[2:06](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/building-great-forms-with-html-and-css/tips-for-adding-search?u=76281980&t=126)** Extending the box to 27 characters would accommodate 90% of queries.
>
> **[2:11](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/building-great-forms-with-html-and-css/tips-for-adding-search?u=76281980&t=131)** If you still need to save space while making a search box prominent, opt for a growing search box.
>
> **[2:18](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/building-great-forms-with-html-and-css/tips-for-adding-search?u=76281980&t=138)** These are going to expand when hovered or clicked.
>
> **[2:21](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/building-great-forms-with-html-and-css/tips-for-adding-search?u=76281980&t=141)** This saves screen space while still giving the user enough visual clues to quickly find and execute a search.
>
> **[2:29](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/building-great-forms-with-html-and-css/tips-for-adding-search?u=76281980&t=149)** As you can see, the search box is a very important criteria.
>
> **[2:33](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/building-great-forms-with-html-and-css/tips-for-adding-search?u=76281980&t=153)** It is going to pay for your end users if you take time to make sure that you're doing search right.

> [!info]- Semantic Content
>
> **Definitions:** is a  (3), is an  (1)
> **CLI Commands:** make (2), find (1)
> **Code Keywords:** function (1)
> **Best Practices:** rule of thumb (1)
> **Warnings:** common mistake (1)

>
> **[2:11](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/building-great-forms-with-html-and-css/add-and-style-a-search-component?u=76281980&t=131)** In this file, I am linking out to some Google Fonts, I'm also linking out to the Font Awesome CDN because I am using some of those icons.
>
> **[2:20](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/building-great-forms-with-html-and-css/add-and-style-a-search-component?u=76281980&t=140)** And you can see that I have an area right here where we're going to put the search bar HTML.
>
> **[2:26](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/building-great-forms-with-html-and-css/add-and-style-a-search-component?u=76281980&t=146)** If you go to the code snippet file, you'll find right at the top our HTML code snippet.
>
> **[2:31](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/building-great-forms-with-html-and-css/add-and-style-a-search-component?u=76281980&t=151)** So I'm going to copy this, we'll go into our HTML file and I'll just replace this with the code that I just copied.
>
> **[2:39](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/building-great-forms-with-html-and-css/add-and-style-a-search-component?u=76281980&t=159)** And let me just add a little bit of indentation by clicking my tab key.
>
> **[2:43](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/building-great-forms-with-html-and-css/add-and-style-a-search-component?u=76281980&t=163)** All right, so I have a couple divs here and I needed some hooks so that I could grab these components together, and then style them in the way that I want.
>
> **[2:52](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/building-great-forms-with-html-and-css/add-and-style-a-search-component?u=76281980&t=172)** So I have a div with a class of swrap.
>
> **[2:56](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/building-great-forms-with-html-and-css/add-and-style-a-search-component?u=76281980&t=176)** Then I have a div with a class of search and I added a role of search here as well.
>
> **[3:01](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/building-great-forms-with-html-and-css/add-and-style-a-search-component?u=76281980&t=181)** This attribute of role is a landmark role.
>
> **[3:05](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/building-great-forms-with-html-and-css/add-and-style-a-search-component?u=76281980&t=185)** Landmarks can be used by assistive technology to quickly identify and navigate to large sections of a document.
>
> **[3:12](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/building-great-forms-with-html-and-css/add-and-style-a-search-component?u=76281980&t=192)** The search role is added to the container element that encompasses the items and the objects that as a whole, combine and create search functionality.
>
> **[3:21](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/building-great-forms-with-html-and-css/add-and-style-a-search-component?u=76281980&t=201)** So when a form is a search form, you want to use the search role on the parent element.
>
> **[3:26](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/building-great-forms-with-html-and-css/add-and-style-a-search-component?u=76281980&t=206)** Like other landmarks, this means it will be listed as a shortcut for most screen readers.
>
> **[3:32](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/building-great-forms-with-html-and-css/add-and-style-a-search-component?u=76281980&t=212)** The extra div is necessary because putting the landmark attribute directly on the form, would override it's semantics.
>
> **[3:40](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/building-great-forms-with-html-and-css/add-and-style-a-search-component?u=76281980&t=220)** Now in many cases, you don't actually need the form tag when you're creating a search element, you could just go ahead and create them with input and button.
>
> **[3:49](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/building-great-forms-with-html-and-css/add-and-style-a-search-component?u=76281980&t=229)** That's not to say that every time you do a search form, you don't have the form element, but in many situations, you won't need it.
>
> **[3:57](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/building-great-forms-with-html-and-css/add-and-style-a-search-component?u=76281980&t=237)** And you can see here I'm not using that.
>
> **[3:59](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/building-great-forms-with-html-and-css/add-and-style-a-search-component?u=76281980&t=239)** In this particular case, I don't want to have a label show.
>
> **[4:04](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/building-great-forms-with-html-and-css/add-and-style-a-search-component?u=76281980&t=244)** A label for this form control is going to help everyone better understand it's purpose.
>
> **[4:09](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/building-great-forms-with-html-and-css/add-and-style-a-search-component?u=76281980&t=249)** But in this case, we're going to be using a visual icon, the search icon, and we're going to have a button and then we'll have our input field where users can enter in the information.
>
> **[4:20](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/building-great-forms-with-html-and-css/add-and-style-a-search-component?u=76281980&t=260)** So because I have no label, I'm using the aria-label attribute.
>
> **[4:26](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/building-great-forms-with-html-and-css/add-and-style-a-search-component?u=76281980&t=266)** The aria-label attribute is used to identify form controls.
>
> **[4:31](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/building-great-forms-with-html-and-css/add-and-style-a-search-component?u=76281980&t=271)** This is well supported with screen readers and other assistive technology.
>
> **[4:36](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/building-great-forms-with-html-and-css/add-and-style-a-search-component?u=76281980&t=276)** But unlike the title attribute, this information is not conveyed to visual users.
>
> **[4:42](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/building-great-forms-with-html-and-css/add-and-style-a-search-component?u=76281980&t=282)** The approach should therefore only be used when that label of the control is clear from the surrounding content.
>

#### Touchscreen fix
> [LinkedIn Learning](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/building-great-forms-with-html-and-css/touchscreen-fix?u=76281980)

> [!transcript]- Transcript
>
> **[0:00](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/building-great-forms-with-html-and-css/touchscreen-fix?u=76281980&t=0)** - [Instructor] If you test your search page on a mobile device, you will find sadly that your cool growing search bar does not work on all mobile browsers.
>
> **[0:10](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/building-great-forms-with-html-and-css/touchscreen-fix?u=76281980&t=10)** Here is the page on Safari, and if I tap on the search bar nothing happens.
>
> **[0:16](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/building-great-forms-with-html-and-css/touchscreen-fix?u=76281980&t=16)** If we go ahead and look at the search bar inside of Chrome, which is showing over here on the right and I tap on the search bar, the search bar is going to work in the way we expect it to.
>
> **[0:30](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/building-great-forms-with-html-and-css/touchscreen-fix?u=76281980&t=30)** So, what gives?
>
> **[0:31](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/building-great-forms-with-html-and-css/touchscreen-fix?u=76281980&t=31)** Why doesn't our search bar work consistently on all mobile browsers?
>
> **[0:36](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/building-great-forms-with-html-and-css/touchscreen-fix?u=76281980&t=36)** Well, our hover behavior is currently on the form element, which is not a traditional hover element.
>
> **[0:43](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/building-great-forms-with-html-and-css/touchscreen-fix?u=76281980&t=43)** Hover will work for touch screens as long as it's a hoverable element, like an image, link or button.
>
> **[0:51](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/building-great-forms-with-html-and-css/touchscreen-fix?u=76281980&t=51)** Today, most web content is designed for keyboard and mouse input.
>
> **[0:55](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/building-great-forms-with-html-and-css/touchscreen-fix?u=76281980&t=55)** However, devices with touch screens, especially portable devices are mainstream, and web applications can either directly process touch-based input by using touch events, or the application can use interpreted mouse events for the application input.
>
> **[1:13](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/building-great-forms-with-html-and-css/touchscreen-fix?u=76281980&t=73)** Luckily, we have access to touch events via JavaScript.
>
> **[1:17](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/building-great-forms-with-html-and-css/touchscreen-fix?u=76281980&t=77)** The touch events interface represents a UI even which is sent when the state of contact with a touch sensitive surface changes.
>
> **[1:25](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/building-great-forms-with-html-and-css/touchscreen-fix?u=76281980&t=85)** This surface can be a touch screen or track pad for example.
>
> **[1:30](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/building-great-forms-with-html-and-css/touchscreen-fix?u=76281980&t=90)** The event can describe one or more points of contact with a screen and includes support for detecting movement, addition and removal of contact points and so forth.
>
> **[1:41](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/building-great-forms-with-html-and-css/touchscreen-fix?u=76281980&t=101)** Touches are represented by the touch object.
>
> **[1:44](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/building-great-forms-with-html-and-css/touchscreen-fix?u=76281980&t=104)** Each touch is described by a position, size and shape, amount of pressure, and the target element.
>
> **[1:51](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/building-great-forms-with-html-and-css/touchscreen-fix?u=76281980&t=111)** There are several types of events that can be fired to indicate that touch-related changes have occurred.
>
> **[1:58](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/building-great-forms-with-html-and-css/touchscreen-fix?u=76281980&t=118)** To resolve our issue, all we have to do is include a little bit of JavaScript.
>
> **[2:03](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/building-great-forms-with-html-and-css/touchscreen-fix?u=76281980&t=123)** Here's the page we've currently been working on, and I'm going to scroll all the way to the bottom.
>
> **[2:08](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/building-great-forms-with-html-and-css/touchscreen-fix?u=76281980&t=128)** We already have some JavaScript on the page which give us a little bit of additional interaction.
>
> **[2:15](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/building-great-forms-with-html-and-css/touchscreen-fix?u=76281980&t=135)** I have a comment down here that says add fix for mobile.

>
> **[2:28](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/building-great-forms-with-html-and-css/touchscreen-fix?u=76281980&t=148)** I'll just paste this here down at the bottom.
>
> **[2:31](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/building-great-forms-with-html-and-css/touchscreen-fix?u=76281980&t=151)** This bit of code is going to allow us to use the touch start event, and it's going to give us the hover effects for touch screen devices.
>
> **[2:41](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/building-great-forms-with-html-and-css/touchscreen-fix?u=76281980&t=161)** A touch start event triggers when the user makes contact with the touch surface and it creates a touch point inside the element that the event is bound to.
>
> **[2:51](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/building-great-forms-with-html-and-css/touchscreen-fix?u=76281980&t=171)** This event can be attached to any element on the page and it's passed as an event object containing details about the touch point such as coordinates on the page.
>
> **[3:03](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/building-great-forms-with-html-and-css/touchscreen-fix?u=76281980&t=183)** So, if we go ahead and save our page and upload this to the web, if I come back to my page and refresh, now when I tap on the search bar it opens as we expect it to, and I'm able to fully use this just as we would anticipate it working.
>
> **[3:22](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/building-great-forms-with-html-and-css/touchscreen-fix?u=76281980&t=202)** In order to provide quality support for touch-based user interfaces, touch events offer the ability to interpret finger or stylus activity on touch screens or track pads.
>
> **[3:34](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/building-great-forms-with-html-and-css/touchscreen-fix?u=76281980&t=214)** And now we have a great looking search element that works equally well on both mobile and desktop devices.

#### Displaying search results
> [LinkedIn Learning](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/building-great-forms-with-html-and-css/displaying-search-results?u=76281980)

> [!transcript]- Transcript
>
> **[0:00](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/building-great-forms-with-html-and-css/displaying-search-results?u=76281980&t=0)** - [Instructor] After helping users input their search data as quickly, easily, and accurately as possible, your goal should now be to deliver the most accurate search results in a legible and easy-to-digest fashion.
>
> **[0:13](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/building-great-forms-with-html-and-css/displaying-search-results?u=76281980&t=13)** The results page is a critical piece of the search experience.
>
> **[0:16](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/building-great-forms-with-html-and-css/displaying-search-results?u=76281980&t=16)** It presents an opportunity to engage a dialogue that guides user's information needs.
>
> **[0:22](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/building-great-forms-with-html-and-css/displaying-search-results?u=76281980&t=22)** Displaying search results is somewhat out of scope of this course about form design, but let's run through some important details.
>
> **[0:30](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/building-great-forms-with-html-and-css/displaying-search-results?u=76281980&t=30)** You'll want to maintain search text.
>
> **[0:32](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/building-great-forms-with-html-and-css/displaying-search-results?u=76281980&t=32)** When the user arrives at a search page, what they typed should persist.
>
> **[0:37](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/building-great-forms-with-html-and-css/displaying-search-results?u=76281980&t=37)** This way users can make tweaks without having to retype the entire query.
>
> **[0:42](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/building-great-forms-with-html-and-css/displaying-search-results?u=76281980&t=42)** Display results count.
>
> **[0:43](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/building-great-forms-with-html-and-css/displaying-search-results?u=76281980&t=43)** Tell users how many results have been returned.
>
> **[0:47](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/building-great-forms-with-html-and-css/displaying-search-results?u=76281980&t=47)** Users can then decide what their next course of action is.
>
> **[0:50](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/building-great-forms-with-html-and-css/displaying-search-results?u=76281980&t=50)** For example, if there are many results, they may decide to filter them.
>
> **[0:54](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/building-great-forms-with-html-and-css/displaying-search-results?u=76281980&t=54)** Let users sort.
>
> **[0:56](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/building-great-forms-with-html-and-css/displaying-search-results?u=76281980&t=56)** Depending on the data set being searched, it's often useful to let users sort by relevance, popularity, or recency, for example.
>
> **[1:05](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/building-great-forms-with-html-and-css/displaying-search-results?u=76281980&t=65)** Don't employ infinite scrolling by default.
>
> **[1:08](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/building-great-forms-with-html-and-css/displaying-search-results?u=76281980&t=68)** This is an anti-pattern with several usability issues.
>
> **[1:12](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/building-great-forms-with-html-and-css/displaying-search-results?u=76281980&t=72)** This leaves "show more" or a standard pagination as an option.
>
> **[1:17](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/building-great-forms-with-html-and-css/displaying-search-results?u=76281980&t=77)** Show more is more appropriate for sites with a lot of user-generated content, where the location of the result is not so important.
>
> **[1:25](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/building-great-forms-with-html-and-css/displaying-search-results?u=76281980&t=85)** Pagination is more appropriate for an e-commerce site where users are looking for a specific item, not just browsing for entertainment.
>
> **[1:33](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/building-great-forms-with-html-and-css/displaying-search-results?u=76281980&t=93)** Select a proper layout for your data.
>
> **[1:36](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/building-great-forms-with-html-and-css/displaying-search-results?u=76281980&t=96)** One of the challenges of displaying search results is that different types of content require different layouts.
>
> **[1:42](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/building-great-forms-with-html-and-css/displaying-search-results?u=76281980&t=102)** Two basic layouts for content presentation are list view and grid view.
>
> **[1:47](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/building-great-forms-with-html-and-css/displaying-search-results?u=76281980&t=107)** A rule of thumb is details in lists, pictures in grids.
>
> **[1:52](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/building-great-forms-with-html-and-css/displaying-search-results?u=76281980&t=112)** Key factor in selecting list view versus grid view is how much information a user needs in order to choose between products.
>
> **[2:00](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/building-great-forms-with-html-and-css/displaying-search-results?u=76281980&t=120)** For products like appliances, where details like model numbers, ratings, and dimensions are major factors in the user's selection process, list view makes more sense.
>
> **[2:10](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/building-great-forms-with-html-and-css/displaying-search-results?u=76281980&t=130)** For clothing and more picture-heavy shopping experiences, the grid view works better.
>
> **[2:16](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/building-great-forms-with-html-and-css/displaying-search-results?u=76281980&t=136)** When dealing with list view, list view has space for many textual elements, such as title, description, and reviews.
>
> **[2:25](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/building-great-forms-with-html-and-css/displaying-search-results?u=76281980&t=145)** It helps users to make a decision based on detailed information.
>
> **[2:29](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/building-great-forms-with-html-and-css/displaying-search-results?u=76281980&t=149)** You'll want to incorporate left-to-right reading, so users can scan the list from left to right.
>
> **[2:36](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/building-great-forms-with-html-and-css/displaying-search-results?u=76281980&t=156)** Put the most important element on the left and provide details to the right.
>
> **[2:40](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/building-great-forms-with-html-and-css/displaying-search-results?u=76281980&t=160)** When dealing with grid view, it's all about images.
>
> **[2:43](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/building-great-forms-with-html-and-css/displaying-search-results?u=76281980&t=163)** Try to limit other elements along with the images.
>
> **[2:47](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/building-great-forms-with-html-and-css/displaying-search-results?u=76281980&t=167)** Users usually can scan four to six grid items at a time.
>
> **[2:51](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/building-great-forms-with-html-and-css/displaying-search-results?u=76281980&t=171)** Making some space around the grid can help users receive information easily.
>
> **[2:56](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/building-great-forms-with-html-and-css/displaying-search-results?u=76281980&t=176)** List view and grid view can both be seen as a group of decision-making elements, such as image, title, reviews, location, et cetera.
>
> **[3:05](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/building-great-forms-with-html-and-css/displaying-search-results?u=76281980&t=185)** The different lies in the layout and the combination of those elements.
>
> **[3:10](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/building-great-forms-with-html-and-css/displaying-search-results?u=76281980&t=190)** This site has a pretty good search experience and a helpful filtering system.
>
> **[3:15](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/building-great-forms-with-html-and-css/displaying-search-results?u=76281980&t=195)** If users start to type a search field, it's going to offer helpful suggestions to aid in speed for the end user.
>
> **[3:22](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/building-great-forms-with-html-and-css/displaying-search-results?u=76281980&t=202)** As soon as I start to type in "pendant lighting," you can see that I have some additional choices that are going to appear.
>
> **[3:30](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/building-great-forms-with-html-and-css/displaying-search-results?u=76281980&t=210)** I'm going to search for pendant lighting.
>
> **[3:32](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/building-great-forms-with-html-and-css/displaying-search-results?u=76281980&t=212)** It's going to take me to the search results page.
>
> **[3:35](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/building-great-forms-with-html-and-css/displaying-search-results?u=76281980&t=215)** As you can see, we have 303 search results for pendant lighting.
>
> **[3:40](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/building-great-forms-with-html-and-css/displaying-search-results?u=76281980&t=220)** We also have a nice filtering system here, so I can filter by type, by color, price, material, and then I can even add more filters.
>
> **[3:50](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/building-great-forms-with-html-and-css/displaying-search-results?u=76281980&t=230)** If we go ahead and start to use some of the filters, the results that we have are going to go down significantly, so you can see, once I add some filters to my search results, I end up with far less results, so I can make a better decision without being overwhelmed with too many choices.
>
> **[4:11](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/building-great-forms-with-html-and-css/displaying-search-results?u=76281980&t=251)** This particular website is going to offer a variety of ways that users can utilize and refine their search.
>
> **[4:19](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/building-great-forms-with-html-and-css/displaying-search-results?u=76281980&t=259)** We can also sort the search by a number of options as well.
>
> **[4:25](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/building-great-forms-with-html-and-css/displaying-search-results?u=76281980&t=265)** This search landing page shows the user an image, a name, color options, gives us a price, and gives us the opportunity to compare the products with something else.
>
> **[4:37](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/building-great-forms-with-html-and-css/displaying-search-results?u=76281980&t=277)** The quick look is going to open up a modal window.
>
> **[4:40](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/building-great-forms-with-html-and-css/displaying-search-results?u=76281980&t=280)** This will give the user even more details and a bigger picture, and if they click this area, they can navigate to a page that is dedicated to sharing all sort of specifications about this particular product.
>
> **[4:53](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/building-great-forms-with-html-and-css/displaying-search-results?u=76281980&t=293)** Carefully considering on how you're going to filter the results is going to become important for your end user.
>
> **[4:59](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/building-great-forms-with-html-and-css/displaying-search-results?u=76281980&t=299)** Always think of them and make the experience as easy and painless as possible.

### 8. Accessibility

#### Form accessibility
> [LinkedIn Learning](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/building-great-forms-with-html-and-css/form-accessibility?u=76281980)

> [!transcript]- Transcript
>
> **[0:00](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/building-great-forms-with-html-and-css/form-accessibility?u=76281980&t=0)** - [Instructor] Forms are an essential component of the web as they connect users to your business and help them accomplish what they came to your site or app for.
>
> **[0:09](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/building-great-forms-with-html-and-css/form-accessibility?u=76281980&t=9)** That being said, you want to make sure that all of your users are able to use your forms without having a horrible experience.
>
> **[0:16](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/building-great-forms-with-html-and-css/form-accessibility?u=76281980&t=16)** The goal is to make these key user interactions as frictionless as possible.
>
> **[0:22](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/building-great-forms-with-html-and-css/form-accessibility?u=76281980&t=22)** You'll want to strive not only to building out good-looking forms, but easy-to-use forms and also make sure that they're accessible.
>
> **[0:29](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/building-great-forms-with-html-and-css/form-accessibility?u=76281980&t=29)** Here are some things to consider when building forms.
>

>

> **[0:48](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/building-great-forms-with-html-and-css/form-accessibility?u=76281980&t=48)** What's accessibility?
>
> **[0:50](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/building-great-forms-with-html-and-css/form-accessibility?u=76281980&t=50)** Accessibility in web design is the ability to design digital products for people with disabilities.
>
> **[0:56](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/building-great-forms-with-html-and-css/form-accessibility?u=76281980&t=56)** Accessibility is not the same as usability.
>
> **[0:59](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/building-great-forms-with-html-and-css/form-accessibility?u=76281980&t=59)** Usability is how users can use a product.
>
> **[1:03](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/building-great-forms-with-html-and-css/form-accessibility?u=76281980&t=63)** Accessibility is more about how people have the ability to access something.
>
> **[1:07](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/building-great-forms-with-html-and-css/form-accessibility?u=76281980&t=67)** Why should web forms be accessible?
>
> **[1:10](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/building-great-forms-with-html-and-css/form-accessibility?u=76281980&t=70)** Sometimes web forms are difficult to complete even without visual or cognitive impairments, so those with disabilities find web forms especially challenging to use.
>
> **[1:20](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/building-great-forms-with-html-and-css/form-accessibility?u=76281980&t=80)** Accessible forms are easier to use for everyone including those with disabilities.
>
> **[1:26](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/building-great-forms-with-html-and-css/form-accessibility?u=76281980&t=86)** People may have cognitive disabilities.
>
> **[1:29](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/building-great-forms-with-html-and-css/form-accessibility?u=76281980&t=89)** Improving the layout structure, instructions, and feedback will lead to a better understanding and the ability to complete web forms.
>
> **[1:37](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/building-great-forms-with-html-and-css/form-accessibility?u=76281980&t=97)** Using speech input, labels through voice commands are used to activate controls and move the focus to the fields that need to be completed.
>
> **[1:45](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/building-great-forms-with-html-and-css/form-accessibility?u=76281980&t=105)** Limited dexterity.
>
> **[1:47](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/building-great-forms-with-html-and-css/form-accessibility?u=76281980&t=107)** Forms with large clickable areas that include labels are easier to navigate.
>
> **[1:52](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/building-great-forms-with-html-and-css/form-accessibility?u=76281980&t=112)** Visual impairments.
>
> **[1:54](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/building-great-forms-with-html-and-css/form-accessibility?u=76281980&t=114)** Forms that are associated with labels are easier to identify and understand.
>
> **[1:59](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/building-great-forms-with-html-and-css/form-accessibility?u=76281980&t=119)** You can make your forms more accessible by making sure your form is keyboard accessible.
>
> **[2:05](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/building-great-forms-with-html-and-css/form-accessibility?u=76281980&t=125)** Ensure that web forms can be used by people who can only navigate your website by a keyboard.
>
> **[2:11](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/building-great-forms-with-html-and-css/form-accessibility?u=76281980&t=131)** Add contrasting colors.
>
> **[2:13](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/building-great-forms-with-html-and-css/form-accessibility?u=76281980&t=133)** Contrasting colors will allow users with visual impairments to identify what fields need to be completed.
>
> **[2:19](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/building-great-forms-with-html-and-css/form-accessibility?u=76281980&t=139)** Using tools that test your website's contrast ratios are a great way to determine if the colors that you've used are appropriate.
>
> **[2:27](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/building-great-forms-with-html-and-css/form-accessibility?u=76281980&t=147)** Then you can make adjustments if there are any sort of inconsistencies.
>
> **[2:31](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/building-great-forms-with-html-and-css/form-accessibility?u=76281980&t=151)** Use labels with form fields and inputs.
>
> **[2:34](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/building-great-forms-with-html-and-css/form-accessibility?u=76281980&t=154)** Ensure that any grouping a form controls are related, so fields are easily understood.
>
> **[2:40](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/building-great-forms-with-html-and-css/form-accessibility?u=76281980&t=160)** Screen readers generally navigate using keyboards and all form labels are read to them as they go through the form.
>
> **[2:47](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/building-great-forms-with-html-and-css/form-accessibility?u=76281980&t=167)** This means that any fields that aren't labeled are going to be skipped.
>
> **[2:50](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/building-great-forms-with-html-and-css/form-accessibility?u=76281980&t=170)** Flag up errors.
>
> **[2:52](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/building-great-forms-with-html-and-css/form-accessibility?u=76281980&t=172)** Make sure that error messages are clear and concise.
>
> **[2:55](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/building-great-forms-with-html-and-css/form-accessibility?u=76281980&t=175)** They should appear where and when the error occurs.
>
> **[3:00](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/building-great-forms-with-html-and-css/form-accessibility?u=76281980&t=180)** Use help text with labels.
>
> **[3:02](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/building-great-forms-with-html-and-css/form-accessibility?u=76281980&t=182)** This is most helpful for those with cognitive disabilities.
>
> **[3:06](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/building-great-forms-with-html-and-css/form-accessibility?u=76281980&t=186)** When a user has gone to search for information that's required in an input field, such a customer number, they may have forgotten that the field was for their customer number.
>
> **[3:16](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/building-great-forms-with-html-and-css/form-accessibility?u=76281980&t=196)** This is why placeholder text isn't as efficient for all users.
>
> **[3:20](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/building-great-forms-with-html-and-css/form-accessibility?u=76281980&t=200)** Help or reminder text should be placed beside the text field label and should be easily accessible.
>
> **[3:27](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/building-great-forms-with-html-and-css/form-accessibility?u=76281980&t=207)** The [w3.org](https://w3.org) has a great comprehensive reference to web accessibility guidelines.
>
> **[3:34](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/building-great-forms-with-html-and-css/form-accessibility?u=76281980&t=214)** If you are more interested in learning about these very important components to web design, I would recommend that you check out this website.
>
> **[3:42](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/building-great-forms-with-html-and-css/form-accessibility?u=76281980&t=222)** It has a wealth of information and there's all sorts of articles and links and examples to things that you can do to improve accessibility as you're building websites.
>
> **[3:54](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/building-great-forms-with-html-and-css/form-accessibility?u=76281980&t=234)** On the web, forms are constantly being used to collect information from users.
>
> **[3:58](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/building-great-forms-with-html-and-css/form-accessibility?u=76281980&t=238)** Signing up for something, buying something, asking a question, or contacting someone.
>
> **[4:03](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/building-great-forms-with-html-and-css/form-accessibility?u=76281980&t=243)** Since forms are so commonly used, it's imperative that we make forms accessible for all users.
>
> **[4:10](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/building-great-forms-with-html-and-css/form-accessibility?u=76281980&t=250)** Making your form accessible will not only ensure that your form will work well for a small group of people, but it's going to improve the experience for every single user.
>
> **[4:19](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/building-great-forms-with-html-and-css/form-accessibility?u=76281980&t=259)** Now, that makes sense.

#### Labeling and describing with ARIA
> [LinkedIn Learning](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/building-great-forms-with-html-and-css/labeling-and-describing-with-aria?u=76281980)

> [!transcript]- Transcript
>
> **[0:00](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/building-great-forms-with-html-and-css/labeling-and-describing-with-aria?u=76281980&t=0)** - [Narrator] As you are developing web-based forms, you should utilize as much accessible technology and tools that you can to ensure the widest accessible experience for all of your users.
>
> **[0:11](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/building-great-forms-with-html-and-css/labeling-and-describing-with-aria?u=76281980&t=11)** ARIA is one way to accomplish this task.
>
> **[0:14](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/building-great-forms-with-html-and-css/labeling-and-describing-with-aria?u=76281980&t=14)** It makes your website more accessible to people with disabilities, such as hearing or visual impairments.
>
> **[0:20](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/building-great-forms-with-html-and-css/labeling-and-describing-with-aria?u=76281980&t=20)** So what is ARIA?
>
> **[0:22](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/building-great-forms-with-html-and-css/labeling-and-describing-with-aria?u=76281980&t=22)** ARIA is shorthand for Accessible Rich Internet Applications.
>
> **[0:26](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/building-great-forms-with-html-and-css/labeling-and-describing-with-aria?u=76281980&t=26)** ARIA is a set of attributes you can add to HTML elements that define ways to make web content and applications accessible to users with disabilities who use assistive technologies.
>
> **[0:37](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/building-great-forms-with-html-and-css/labeling-and-describing-with-aria?u=76281980&t=37)** When accessibility issues cannot be managed with native HTML, ARIA can help bridge those gaps.
>
> **[0:44](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/building-great-forms-with-html-and-css/labeling-and-describing-with-aria?u=76281980&t=44)** There are two main components used in ARIA, roles and attributes.
>
> **[0:49](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/building-great-forms-with-html-and-css/labeling-and-describing-with-aria?u=76281980&t=49)** ARIA roles are added to HTML markup like an attribute.
>
> **[0:53](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/building-great-forms-with-html-and-css/labeling-and-describing-with-aria?u=76281980&t=53)** They define the type of element and suggest what purpose it serves.
>
> **[0:57](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/building-great-forms-with-html-and-css/labeling-and-describing-with-aria?u=76281980&t=57)** In this example, we are using the ARIA role on the div element to help identify this section of the page as a form.
>
> **[1:04](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/building-great-forms-with-html-and-css/labeling-and-describing-with-aria?u=76281980&t=64)** It is worth noting that if you are using a form tag then you would not need the ARIA portion, since HTML five semantics provides meaning enough.
>
> **[1:13](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/building-great-forms-with-html-and-css/labeling-and-describing-with-aria?u=76281980&t=73)** Whenever possible, try to use the native HTML five tags rather than using ARIA.
>
> **[1:19](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/building-great-forms-with-html-and-css/labeling-and-describing-with-aria?u=76281980&t=79)** ARIA attributes are slightly different from roles.
>
> **[1:23](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/building-great-forms-with-html-and-css/labeling-and-describing-with-aria?u=76281980&t=83)** They are added to markup in the same way, but there is a range of ARIA attributes available for use.
>
> **[1:29](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/building-great-forms-with-html-and-css/labeling-and-describing-with-aria?u=76281980&t=89)** All ARIA attributes are prefixed with aria dash.
>
> **[1:33](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/building-great-forms-with-html-and-css/labeling-and-describing-with-aria?u=76281980&t=93)** There are two types of attributes, states and properties.
>
> **[1:37](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/building-great-forms-with-html-and-css/labeling-and-describing-with-aria?u=76281980&t=97)** The value of states are bound to change as a result of user interaction.
>
> **[1:41](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/building-great-forms-with-html-and-css/labeling-and-describing-with-aria?u=76281980&t=101)** The value of properties is less likely to change.
>
> **[1:45](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/building-great-forms-with-html-and-css/labeling-and-describing-with-aria?u=76281980&t=105)** The first and most important role of ARIA is to use native HTML at all times unless it's absolutely, positively impossible to make an element accessible otherwise.
>
> **[1:56](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/building-great-forms-with-html-and-css/labeling-and-describing-with-aria?u=76281980&t=116)** When in doubt, choose native HTML.
>
> **[1:59](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/building-great-forms-with-html-and-css/labeling-and-describing-with-aria?u=76281980&t=119)** Do not change native HTML semantics unless you absolutely have to.
>
> **[2:03](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/building-great-forms-with-html-and-css/labeling-and-describing-with-aria?u=76281980&t=123)** In almost all cases, native HTML will work just fine.
>
> **[2:07](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/building-great-forms-with-html-and-css/labeling-and-describing-with-aria?u=76281980&t=127)** This is because ARIA does not get added to the document model, so any objects created in the browser will not automatically become what they are trying to emulate.
>
> **[2:17](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/building-great-forms-with-html-and-css/labeling-and-describing-with-aria?u=76281980&t=137)** All interactive ARIA controls must be keyboard accessible.
>
> **[2:21](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/building-great-forms-with-html-and-css/labeling-and-describing-with-aria?u=76281980&t=141)** For elements that don't normally receive keyboard focus, add tab index equals zero to the element and add it to the logical tab order.
>
> **[2:30](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/building-great-forms-with-html-and-css/labeling-and-describing-with-aria?u=76281980&t=150)** For all elements that are focusable, do not ever add role presentation or aria hidden true.
>
> **[2:37](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/building-great-forms-with-html-and-css/labeling-and-describing-with-aria?u=76281980&t=157)** This is because for some users this will result in them focusing on nothing, causing confusion.
>
> **[2:43](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/building-great-forms-with-html-and-css/labeling-and-describing-with-aria?u=76281980&t=163)** Elements that are automatically focusable include links and form elements.
>
> **[2:47](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/building-great-forms-with-html-and-css/labeling-and-describing-with-aria?u=76281980&t=167)** Any element that has a tab index of zero added to it is also included in that list.
>
> **[2:53](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/building-great-forms-with-html-and-css/labeling-and-describing-with-aria?u=76281980&t=173)** You must give all interactive elements an accessible name.
>
> **[2:57](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/building-great-forms-with-html-and-css/labeling-and-describing-with-aria?u=76281980&t=177)** I'll show you what this means in an upcoming exercise.
>
> **[3:00](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/building-great-forms-with-html-and-css/labeling-and-describing-with-aria?u=76281980&t=180)** To make forms accessible for users of screen readers, add ARIA attributes to the form elements so that they become easier to interact with if necessary.

> [!info]- Semantic Content
>
> **Env Vars:** aria (17), html (9)
> **CLI Commands:** make (3)
> **Definitions:** is a  (2)
> **Analogies:** such as (1)

>

>
> **[7:40](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/building-great-forms-with-html-and-css/using-aria?u=76281980&t=460)** As developers, let's strive to make life easier for them and everyone.

### 9. Submission

#### Client-side form validation
> [LinkedIn Learning](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/building-great-forms-with-html-and-css/client-side-form-validation?u=76281980)

> [!transcript]- Transcript
>
> **[0:00](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/building-great-forms-with-html-and-css/client-side-form-validation?u=76281980&t=0)** - [Instructor] Before submitting data to the server, it's important to ensure all required form controls are filled out in the correct format.
>
> **[0:07](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/building-great-forms-with-html-and-css/client-side-form-validation?u=76281980&t=7)** This is called client-side form validation and it helps ensure data submitted matches the requirements set forth in the various form controls.
>
> **[0:16](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/building-great-forms-with-html-and-css/client-side-form-validation?u=76281980&t=16)** Client-side validation is an initial check and an important feature of good user experience.
>
> **[0:22](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/building-great-forms-with-html-and-css/client-side-form-validation?u=76281980&t=22)** By catching invalid data on the client's side, the user can fix it straight away.
>
> **[0:27](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/building-great-forms-with-html-and-css/client-side-form-validation?u=76281980&t=27)** If it gets to the server and is then rejected, a delay is caused by a round trip to the server and then back to the client side to tell the user how to fix the data.
>
> **[0:37](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/building-great-forms-with-html-and-css/client-side-form-validation?u=76281980&t=37)** They may or may not be there at that point, so this doesn't make for great user experience.
>
> **[0:43](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/building-great-forms-with-html-and-css/client-side-form-validation?u=76281980&t=43)** Many websites with registration forms provide feedback when you don't enter your data in the format that they are expecting.
>
> **[0:50](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/building-great-forms-with-html-and-css/client-side-form-validation?u=76281980&t=50)** You'll get a message such as, the field is required, you can't leave this field blank, please enter your phone number in the format of, please enter a valid email address, your password needs to be between eight and 17 characters and contain one upper case and one symbol.
>
> **[1:05](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/building-great-forms-with-html-and-css/client-side-form-validation?u=76281980&t=65)** All of this is called form validation.
>
> **[1:08](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/building-great-forms-with-html-and-css/client-side-form-validation?u=76281980&t=68)** When you enter data, the browser, and or the web server, will check to see that the data is in the correct format and within the constraints set by the application.
>
> **[1:18](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/building-great-forms-with-html-and-css/client-side-form-validation?u=76281980&t=78)** Validation done in the browser is called client-side validation, while validation done on the server is called server-side validation.
>
> **[1:25](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/building-great-forms-with-html-and-css/client-side-form-validation?u=76281980&t=85)** In this course, we are focusing on client-side validation.
>
> **[1:29](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/building-great-forms-with-html-and-css/client-side-form-validation?u=76281980&t=89)** If the information is correctly formatted, the application allows the data to be submitted to the server and usually saved in a database.
>
> **[1:37](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/building-great-forms-with-html-and-css/client-side-form-validation?u=76281980&t=97)** If the information isn't correctly formatted, it gives the user an error message explaining what needs to be corrected and lets them try again.
>
> **[1:45](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/building-great-forms-with-html-and-css/client-side-form-validation?u=76281980&t=105)** There are two different types of client-side validation that you'll encounter on the web, built-in form validation that uses HTML5 form validation features, which we've discussed in many places throughout this course.
>
> **[1:58](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/building-great-forms-with-html-and-css/client-side-form-validation?u=76281980&t=118)** This validation generally doesn't require much JavaScript.
>
> **[2:02](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/building-great-forms-with-html-and-css/client-side-form-validation?u=76281980&t=122)** Built-in form validation has better performance, in many cases, than JavaScript, but it is not as customizable as a JavaScript validation.
>
> **[2:11](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/building-great-forms-with-html-and-css/client-side-form-validation?u=76281980&t=131)** The second type of client-side validation is JavaScript validation.
>
> **[2:15](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/building-great-forms-with-html-and-css/client-side-form-validation?u=76281980&t=135)** This is obviously coded using JavaScript.
>
> **[2:18](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/building-great-forms-with-html-and-css/client-side-form-validation?u=76281980&t=138)** This validation is completely customizable, but you'll have to create it all or use a preexisting library.
>
> **[2:25](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/building-great-forms-with-html-and-css/client-side-form-validation?u=76281980&t=145)** One of the most significant features of HTML5 form controls is the ability to validate most user data without relying on JavaScript.
>
> **[2:33](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/building-great-forms-with-html-and-css/client-side-form-validation?u=76281980&t=153)** This is done by using validation attributes on form elements.
>
> **[2:37](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/building-great-forms-with-html-and-css/client-side-form-validation?u=76281980&t=157)** We've seen many of these earlier on in the course, but just to recap we have required, this is specifying whether a form field needs to be filled in before the form can be submitted.
>
> **[2:48](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/building-great-forms-with-html-and-css/client-side-form-validation?u=76281980&t=168)** Midlength and maxlength, they specify the minimum and maximum length of textual data.
>
> **[2:54](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/building-great-forms-with-html-and-css/client-side-form-validation?u=76281980&t=174)** Min max, these specify the minimum and maximum values of numerical input types.
>
> **[3:00](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/building-great-forms-with-html-and-css/client-side-form-validation?u=76281980&t=180)** Type specifies whether the data needs to be a number, an email address, or some other specific preset type.
>
> **[3:06](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/building-great-forms-with-html-and-css/client-side-form-validation?u=76281980&t=186)** And pattern, which specifies a regular expression that defines a pattern that the entered data needs to follow.
>
> **[3:13](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/building-great-forms-with-html-and-css/client-side-form-validation?u=76281980&t=193)** If the data entered into a form field follows any of the rules specified by the above attributes, it is considered valid.
>
> **[3:20](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/building-great-forms-with-html-and-css/client-side-form-validation?u=76281980&t=200)** If not, it is invalid.
>
> **[3:22](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/building-great-forms-with-html-and-css/client-side-form-validation?u=76281980&t=202)** Let's look at how we can incorporate some client-side validation into our various forms.

>

>

>

>
> **[7:23](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/building-great-forms-with-html-and-css/javascript-validation?u=76281980&t=443)** So as I enter the information, you can see, that if I've entered an uppercase, I get the check and it turns green, if I enter lowercase, again, I get the check and it turns green.
>
> **[7:34](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/building-great-forms-with-html-and-css/javascript-validation?u=76281980&t=454)** Here, I've entered a number, and I'm going to add a special character so I've met four of the five criteria, if I try to submit it's going to let me know that hey, this needs to be between eight and 100 characters.
>
> **[7:46](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/building-great-forms-with-html-and-css/javascript-validation?u=76281980&t=466)** So I can go ahead and remedy that problem, and then I'll be able to submit this.
>
> **[7:52](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/building-great-forms-with-html-and-css/javascript-validation?u=76281980&t=472)** With client side validation, the form never gets submitted if the validation fails.
>
> **[7:58](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/building-great-forms-with-html-and-css/javascript-validation?u=76281980&t=478)** validation in this case is being handled With the JavaScript methods that we created, or it might be a framework or plugins, and users are going to get immediate feedback if the validation fails.
>
> **[8:10](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/building-great-forms-with-html-and-css/javascript-validation?u=76281980&t=490)** The best case scenario for validation is to pair client side validation with server side validation.
>
> **[8:17](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/building-great-forms-with-html-and-css/javascript-validation?u=76281980&t=497)** By combining the methods will get the best of the two.
>
> **[8:21](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/building-great-forms-with-html-and-css/javascript-validation?u=76281980&t=501)** A faster response, and then more secure validation and better user experience.

#### Good error messages
> [LinkedIn Learning](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/building-great-forms-with-html-and-css/good-error-messages?u=76281980)

> [!transcript]- Transcript
>
> **[0:00](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/building-great-forms-with-html-and-css/good-error-messages?u=76281980&t=0)** - [Narrator] No system can work without errors.
>
> **[0:03](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/building-great-forms-with-html-and-css/good-error-messages?u=76281980&t=3)** It could be a user's errors or a system failure.
>
> **[0:06](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/building-great-forms-with-html-and-css/good-error-messages?u=76281980&t=6)** In either case, it's very important to handle errors in the right way, as they are very crucial for a good user experience.
>
> **[0:13](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/building-great-forms-with-html-and-css/good-error-messages?u=76281980&t=13)** There are three vital parts to every good error message: clear text message, right placement, good visual design.
>
> **[0:22](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/building-great-forms-with-html-and-css/good-error-messages?u=76281980&t=22)** Your error message should be clear.
>
> **[0:24](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/building-great-forms-with-html-and-css/good-error-messages?u=76281980&t=24)** The error message should clearly define what the problem is, why it happened and what to do about it.
>
> **[0:30](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/building-great-forms-with-html-and-css/good-error-messages?u=76281980&t=30)** Think of your error message as a conversation with your user.
>
> **[0:33](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/building-great-forms-with-html-and-css/good-error-messages?u=76281980&t=33)** It should sound like it's been written for humans.
>
> **[0:36](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/building-great-forms-with-html-and-css/good-error-messages?u=76281980&t=36)** Make sure your error message is polite, understandable, friendly, and jargon-free.
>
> **[0:42](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/building-great-forms-with-html-and-css/good-error-messages?u=76281980&t=42)** Error messages should be helpful, it's not enough to write that something went wrong.
>
> **[0:47](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/building-great-forms-with-html-and-css/good-error-messages?u=76281980&t=47)** Show the users how to fix it as soon and as easy as possible.
>
> **[0:50](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/building-great-forms-with-html-and-css/good-error-messages?u=76281980&t=50)** For example, here we're describing what's wrong and providing a solution in the error message so that you can immediately fix the issue.
>
> **[0:59](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/building-great-forms-with-html-and-css/good-error-messages?u=76281980&t=59)** Error messages should be specific to a situation.
>
> **[1:02](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/building-great-forms-with-html-and-css/good-error-messages?u=76281980&t=62)** Very often, websites use only one error message for all validation states.
>
> **[1:08](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/building-great-forms-with-html-and-css/good-error-messages?u=76281980&t=68)** You left this email field blank.
>
> **[1:10](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/building-great-forms-with-html-and-css/good-error-messages?u=76281980&t=70)** It might say, enter a valid email address.
>
> **[1:12](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/building-great-forms-with-html-and-css/good-error-messages?u=76281980&t=72)** You missed the at character.
>
> **[1:14](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/building-great-forms-with-html-and-css/good-error-messages?u=76281980&t=74)** Enter a valid email address.
>
> **[1:16](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/building-great-forms-with-html-and-css/good-error-messages?u=76281980&t=76)** Show your users actual messages instead of giving general ones.
>
> **[1:21](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/building-great-forms-with-html-and-css/good-error-messages?u=76281980&t=81)** Error messages should be polite.
>
> **[1:23](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/building-great-forms-with-html-and-css/good-error-messages?u=76281980&t=83)** Don't blame your users that they did something wrong, even if they did.
>
> **[1:27](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/building-great-forms-with-html-and-css/good-error-messages?u=76281980&t=87)** Be polite to your user.
>
> **[1:29](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/building-great-forms-with-html-and-css/good-error-messages?u=76281980&t=89)** Make them feel comfortable and convenient.
>
> **[1:31](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/building-great-forms-with-html-and-css/good-error-messages?u=76281980&t=91)** It's a great opportunity to use your brand voice and add personality to the errors.
>
> **[1:36](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/building-great-forms-with-html-and-css/good-error-messages?u=76281980&t=96)** Use humor when it's appropriate.
>
> **[1:39](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/building-great-forms-with-html-and-css/good-error-messages?u=76281980&t=99)** Be careful using humor in your error messages.
>
> **[1:42](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/building-great-forms-with-html-and-css/good-error-messages?u=76281980&t=102)** First of all, the error message should be informative and helpful.
>
> **[1:45](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/building-great-forms-with-html-and-css/good-error-messages?u=76281980&t=105)** Then you can improve user experience by adding some humor in your error message if it's appropriate.
>
> **[1:51](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/building-great-forms-with-html-and-css/good-error-messages?u=76281980&t=111)** A good error message is one that you can see when you need it.
>
> **[1:55](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/building-great-forms-with-html-and-css/good-error-messages?u=76281980&t=115)** Avoid error summaries, place error messages next to the UI elements that they're related to.
>
> **[2:01](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/building-great-forms-with-html-and-css/good-error-messages?u=76281980&t=121)** The error message should be clearly visible.
>
> **[2:04](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/building-great-forms-with-html-and-css/good-error-messages?u=76281980&t=124)** Use contrasting text and background colors so the user can easily notice and read the message.
>
> **[2:10](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/building-great-forms-with-html-and-css/good-error-messages?u=76281980&t=130)** It's very common that red is used for error messages.
>
> **[2:14](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/building-great-forms-with-html-and-css/good-error-messages?u=76281980&t=134)** In some cases, it may be yellow or orange.
>
> **[2:17](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/building-great-forms-with-html-and-css/good-error-messages?u=76281980&t=137)** Whatever color you decide to use, make sure that the error text is legible with noticeable contrast against its background color.
>
> **[2:26](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/building-great-forms-with-html-and-css/good-error-messages?u=76281980&t=146)** Don't forget to provide a related icon alongside the color to improve the accessibility for people that are color blind.
>
> **[2:33](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/building-great-forms-with-html-and-css/good-error-messages?u=76281980&t=153)** Creating helpful and concise error messages are often overlooked, but it's important for an optimal user experience.
>
> **[2:40](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/building-great-forms-with-html-and-css/good-error-messages?u=76281980&t=160)** Error messages are a great opportunity to improve user experience, share your brand, voice and personality.
>
> **[2:47](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/building-great-forms-with-html-and-css/good-error-messages?u=76281980&t=167)** Pay attention to all aspects of a good error message, the language, placement, and the visual design to make it really perfect.

#### Success page
> [LinkedIn Learning](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/building-great-forms-with-html-and-css/success-page?u=76281980)

> [!transcript]- Transcript
>
> **[0:00](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/building-great-forms-with-html-and-css/success-page?u=76281980&t=0)** - [Narrator] A form success message can be a complete game changer in terms of customer retention, loyalty and trust.
>
> **[0:07](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/building-great-forms-with-html-and-css/success-page?u=76281980&t=7)** You need to communicate and be clear on what's going to happen next.
>
> **[0:11](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/building-great-forms-with-html-and-css/success-page?u=76281980&t=11)** After all, your users took time out of their day to fill out your form, at least make sure they know that this was done correctly.
>
> **[0:18](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/building-great-forms-with-html-and-css/success-page?u=76281980&t=18)** Don't leave your users hanging, unsure if the form was actually submitted.
>
> **[0:22](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/building-great-forms-with-html-and-css/success-page?u=76281980&t=22)** Your web form is not just a means by which you can collect data and pester your customers with emails afterwards.
>
> **[0:29](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/building-great-forms-with-html-and-css/success-page?u=76281980&t=29)** Every form submission is in fact a bridge of communication you established between you and your future customer.
>
> **[0:36](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/building-great-forms-with-html-and-css/success-page?u=76281980&t=36)** And your form fields are nothing but little signals you give your customer that you are there ready to talk.
>
> **[0:42](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/building-great-forms-with-html-and-css/success-page?u=76281980&t=42)** Your success message may be a new page or it could be more of an AJAX or modal window type of interaction that just refreshes in the current page.
>
> **[0:51](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/building-great-forms-with-html-and-css/success-page?u=76281980&t=51)** When it comes to form success message, there's only one basic rule to follow, when the user clicks submit, you need to show you appreciate their input, their questions, their engagement.
>
> **[1:02](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/building-great-forms-with-html-and-css/success-page?u=76281980&t=62)** A successful message is perhaps one of the essential elements to consider when it comes to ensuring that your form leaves your user feeling satisfied and confident that their interaction has been received and some sort of action on your part is being taken.
